THE MED PAGE.
Lawson’s Prose.
Angus and Robertson reprint by permission and
issue in admirable form Lawson’s prose tales and
sketches from The Bulletin and other papers
l odpp., ctoth-bcmm!, mailed free from Bulletin
oitice to any address in Australasia far 5s. 6d. in
Btamps or post-notes).
* ,• • •
Certainly no complaint has to be made on tho
score of quantity : there is some as to quality, and
* good deal as to arrangement.
Many of Lawson’s sketches are written in
series,: there is a ‘"Mitchell ” sequence, a “Steel-
man sequence, a bush sequence, a city sequence,
and so on. The obvious way of dealing with
these was the best way. They should have been
classified and put in sections, so that continuity
might be unbroken and the characters might gain
force and distinctness from the massing of impres-
sions. Exactly the opposite course has been fol-
lowed. The book is like a bad cook’s ragoht.
You get here a mouthful of salt, there one of
pepper, the next is meat uncondimented. Not
? , Y V* Power lost, but the haphazard mixture
jolts the mind like an unexpected bottom-step.
The pity of it is greater because
with the best arrangement the
matter would seem scrappy and
disconnected. Written for occa-
sional publication, these detached
sketches have no unity of idea
or treatment. Their appeal was
bound to be diffused and vague :
the least that could be done was
to concentrate the interest as far
as the slight skeins of similarity
permitted, and instead of half-
a-hundred taps to strike half-a-
dozen blows. It was not done.
Lawson might conceivably have
written many of his fragmentary
impressions into a single plotted,
climaxed story which would make
a permanent mark. Or if even
he had contrived a set of charac-
ters to pass from chapter to
chapter, as Mark Twain manages,
and hung his matter on their
pegs, his result would have been
stronger. As it is, the reader
is perpetually getting-up steam
for a five-minutes’ journey which
brings him back to starting-point.
And only half-a-dozen of these
sketches—as printed—are some-
thing like literature. The rest are
frequently good journal - work,
good material for literature—no-
thing more.
* * *
Yet the standard for criticism
of Lawson is rising as he rises.
We ask from him better tilings
than we asked a year ago. Hither-
to he has only had to make a
reputation-with his endowments
an easy task : now—O labor of giants ! —he has
to maintain it.
And Lawson’s public reception has been quite
equal to his deserts. Even iiis first poor booklet
(printed by his mother, Louisa Lawson) met praise
instant and generous; his volume of poems has
been noticed by a hundred critics anxious to forget
blemishes, keen to discover beauties; the rolling
snowball of his fame has been kicked onward by a
thousand eager feet. Thanks in part to this zealous
ardor, there is not a corner of Australia where his
name is unknown ; not a town in which he will
not find some strange hand stretched to «rip his,
some honest face shining with pleasure to greet
the poet of the bush.
What a change since Gordon’s day ! When
that poor baffled idealist blew his brains out in
the covert of the Brighton scrub, his talent was
hardly known, certainly unappreciated by the
mass of Australasian readers. Clarke says he
died “in the full flood of his success, with con-
gratulations pouring upon him from every side.”
But Clarke referred all things to the standard of
Collins-street. His world was Melbourne, with
an occasional extension to the Wimmera bush.
And outside Melbourne—or, on a liberal estimate,
Victoria—Gordon at his death was practically
unheard-of. A few sportsmen, a few literati,
made the circle of his admirers: his fame is
purely posthumous. The quickening of the Aus-
tralian literary sense in the past ten years is won-
derful to look back upon. Now, if there are not
many to detect new talent, there are very many
able to appreciate it when detected and ready to
chorus enthusiastic welcome.
Of this quickening Lawson and Paterson and
the others of the new bevy of bards and writers
are reaping the fruits. But reputation, like
rank, has its responsibilities. If Lawson’s is not
to wane, he must wax continually.
* * * *
The charm of Lawson's prose is essentially that
of his poetry. Art he has none; his artifices
are of the feeblest. For the most part he might
say with Antony ;
I only speak right on ;
I tell you that which you yourselves do know.
But what others merely know, Lawson feels. He
is indeed abnormally sensitive : the trifles which
make evanescent impression on ordinary minds
draw blood (and ink) from his. Then ordinary
minds with pleasure recognise their own impres-
sions. “ Why, these are our thoughts; these
people are our people ; these scenes and places are
the scenes and places we have known for all our
lives.”
Precisely ; yet until Lawson pictured, revealed,
and vitalised them, those thoughts, those people,
those places and scenes never really existed for
ordinary minds. Which is, of course, but a varia-
tion on the well-worn apothegms that beauty is in
the eye of the gazer, that we get out of everything
exactly what we put into it, that one man’s meat
is another man’s poison, and the rest. The ordi-
nary mind lives all a life behind horn windows :
genius has invented glass. Its senses are acute to
the point of ecstasy; it sees the rays of the human
spectrum to grosser eyes invisible ; like the beau-
tiful Norse god Baldur, it can hear the grass
growing. And Lawson, as before enunciated, has
a touch of genius.
His instincts of assimilation and selection are
“ The y have been toeing about her for three hours." (Illustrating “ A Day on a Selection" in Lawson's
book of prose sketches.)
matched in his later work by his instinct of ex-
pression. His quaint simple style suits his
rhemes and mode of thought. And his manner is
strengthening. The happy word and phrase come
to him easily : the incidents fall without effort
into place : his picture is made before he knows.
Lawson is beginning to find himself.
Yet his impressionable mind often reflects the
color of the medium through which it has been
flowing last. There are hints of Bret Harte and
Dickens in his earlier work ; of Mark Twain and
Bulletin humorists in his later. And the work
is none the better for them. Regard for Lawson’s
reputation would, indeed, have excised a good
half of the book—especially stories in which the
pathos is deliberately manufactured, like “ Arvie
Aspinall’s Alarm Clock ” ; and valueless sketches
like “ Bogg of Geebung,” with similar imagina-
tive effects ; and undistinguished journalism like
“For Auld Lang Syne.” The remainder, in their
class, would be very good indeed : asitis, thelevel
of the volume is a long way below its best pages.
Not the best, but the most promising are those,
which tell “An Unfinished Love Story.’’ Here,
for the first time, Lawson ceases to describe
characteristics and starts to create ciiaracters.
* * * *
Mahony’s drawings are mostly good, and they
really illustrate the priut. Two, by permission of
the publishers, are reproduced in this issue of
The Bulletin. And, as it has not been said
before, it may be said now that “While the
Billy Boils ’’—which is the disconnected name of
Lawson’s disconnected book—is chock-full of in-
terest for Australasian readers, and worth five
shillings of any man’s or woman’s money. And
it will be easy for intelligent people themselves to
make the quotations which, did space permit,
would here be given to justify and elucidate these
diota.
Is nob the intellectual cream of Melbourne sup-
posed to rise near '‘The Book-Lovers’Library ” —
whose list of August-added books is nearly all
novels, most bad ones ?
Notes.
Sydney’s new journal, The Block, is very
nicely printed, but it is not the model paper. For
one thing, it is not cocksure and dogmatic enough.
When the millionaire is found (“ In the beginning
was a millionaire ” will be the first line of the
model paper’s gospel) and the model paper is
founded, cocksure dogma will be of its essence.
That is to say, it will so consistently use its own
thinker to do its own thinking that the enormous
majority of persons who mentally live by fifth-
hand opinions alone will be irregularly knocked
flat by the wind of its fluttering pages. Irregu-
larly—for the model newspaper will be movable
in space and time: the millionaire alone will be
fixed. That is to say, when there are no topics
and the staff has no ideas the paper will stop issue
until ideas and topics come along, and even at
other times its size will range from a leaflet to a
volume.
Further, the model newspaper will go on circuit
like the law-courts. There is rarely more than
one topic of leading national importance on the
carpet at one time, and the model paper will seek
that topic where it may be found, and fall upon it
while it is yet near. The editor and his assistants
will migrate to Melbourne, or Auckland, or
Hobart—or wherever the Dean case of the day
may be—and be in readiness to write up the
Nocent Waterspout on the spot, eftsoons as it
rises. Of course, the model newspaper will not
chronicle the petty currencies of local gossip, or
discuss the petty problems of local interest. ’ It
will employ the best minds of the day on national
questions and permanent questions, and its
articles will be signed, and its writers will say
what they darn well please and take the responsi-
bility as long as the matter’s interesting. And—
many other things, all dependent on the million-
aire. Can anybody oblige with a millionaire?
The recent Burns eruption threw curious light
on the periodicity of the human mind. It is
generally recognised that before you can draw a
bucketful of water up the ordinary pump you
must pour a dipperful of water down. Similarly
unless you bribe the ordinary intellect with an
anniversary, you may pump till your head is tired
without getting one responsive idea. Put a cen-
tenary in the slot, and the populace will work.
The peculiarity is plainly an aboriginal trait —
the mark of a juvenile stage of individual- and
race-development. You meet it in the woman
who repeats obituary poetry in the papers on the
anniversary of some relative’s cjeath, and Woman
is nearest the juvenile and aboriginal type. And
you are confirmed in the diagnosis of it by recol-
lecting that anniversaries such as birthdays and
wedding-days are most significant to children and
women the most intellectually backward among
individuals; while anniversaries such as Sunday
and Christmas are most significant to the most in-
tellectually backward M i>•< > n rr races.
And, indeed, from in, cultured point of view,
a death-centenary would seem a gruesome cele-
bration, were not the instinct of its gruesomeness
dominated by the more deeply-rooted instinct of
its anmversariness. From a cultured point of
view, there is absolutely no reason why Robert
Burns should be specially remembered at the end
of 100 years rather than at the end of 101, or 99, or
58, or any other arbitrary period of what we call
time. And, goingstill further, if Burns had really a
message of value, every cultured mind should
have absorbed it and be permeated with it all the
time, and the significance of anniversaries would
be lost altogether, since every moment would be
as significant as every other.
This follows from the doctrine of association of
ideas. The wiser a man grows, and the more ex-
tended his culture, the larger becomes his stock of
concepts, and the more numerous the connections
between them and the images presented to his
mind. Thus, a baby looking at a flower may per-
ceive that it is red. An uncultured man may
know that it is red and round and odorous and is
called a rose. Alice adds that it is good to wear
and matches her gown ; Marmaduke that it costs
sixpence at the florist’s; and so on. But a cul-
tured man knows all that literature has said about
roses; he reads in the blushing petals the poetry of
humanity, and hears as he gazes the ecstatic song
of the bulbul:
Sweet, ever sweeter, sweetest Love hath been:
Shirin, Shirintar, 0 Shirintarin I
And the really wise man, looking, sees and is
reminded of so much more that it is evident that
at the apex of culture and the extremity of wis-
dom to which we hope we are tending the least
mental impression derived from any source will
suffice by association of ideas to callnip the whole
thinkable uni verse from the storehouses of memory
and imagination. So that we are really binding
ourselves to a lower race-standard when we worship
days and times and seasons, and we make a crab-
like and degenerate leap by merely honoring our
poets a hundred years after they die instead of
bringing them into our hearts and living with
them and honoring them every moment.
P.S.—Perhaps Burns is an exception, though :
think of Haggis all the time !
P.P.S.—No, lie isn’t; think of whisky all the
time—the compulsion to celebrate a Burns anni-
versary (and all the other anniversaries) every
moment! The imagination here reels.
[Plainly, it is time to lock the imagination up
as drunk and disorderly.]
Several correspondents testify to the existence
of another privileged ring at Melb. Public
Library. “Atone time files of periodicals were
repeatedly short of last numbers, in consequence
of trustee leader-writers borrowing them; and
books were constantly missing for the same
reason.” And, “looking for any attractive book,
you find continually that it has disappeared in
hocus-pocus fashion. Not long ago I missed ‘La
Pucelle/ ‘ The Scarlet Letter ’ and ‘ Tom Jones.’”

BEFORE YOU
T. 33 A FI
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and 0,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks, ” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round —
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Stated aldermanically that the 3000 or so
dogs destroyed annually by the Sydney police go
to the corporation “ tips ” along with the decayed
vegetables, old rags, rotten leather, and general
street-sweepings. The corporation “tips” are on
low-lying city and suburban areas that are being
“ reclaimed ” (save the mark!). On these . areas,
new terraces of houses, generally jerry-built, are
erected, and swarming families in search of cheap
rents take out the advantages thus offered them in
fever-epidemics, and spread the malaria of dead-
dog on a “ God-fearingly-governed ” metropolis.
“ Reclaim the land ! ” Dead dog, be it avowed,
By any other name would stink as loud.
“'The friend of man ! a mangy pest with breath,
And still more pestilential after death.
Beware the bark-stilled sausage at your lips !
Hide the dog-carrion at the garbage-tips !
Mange and hydatids earn a living ban ;
And when defunct, ’bis still “the friend of man.

The Campfires of the Lost.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Who will may see, on plains around
By scanty rivers crossed,
Where none but weedy growths abound,
The campfires of the Lost.
To fan the blaze, the twigs and cones
From dying Hopes we tear ;
And wolfish Angers gnaw the bones
Of dead Ideals there.
And effigies of sacred things,
Or bric-a-brac of Fame,
Anon a stern-lipped watcher flings
Remorseless to the flame.
To drown your glory in the dark,
O, children of the Light !
The frail, the crushed, the fell, the stark
Deploy their hosts to-night.
Grim scouts o’erlcap your city’s walls,
Oast potions in your wells,
With leprous patches taint your halls,
And mine your citadels.
Your timid treasurers await
The onset of our need ;
The .myriad tramp his lonely hate
Is whetting with his greed.
Your serfs now mocking greet your cries
Of “ honor,” “ law,” and “ trust ” ;
Your lily women recognise
The prowling lips of Lust.
Your veil of Art, by free winds tossed,
Is rending as you look—
Your Art, which claimed to love the Lost,
And jeered them, and forsook.
Your brutal Science yields a corps
Of derelicts, to train
With formulas of lethal lore
Our nascent rebel brain.
The scavengers of Learning there,
And outcaste lords of rhyme
Compose us anthems of despair,
And polyglots of crime.
And godless phalanxes assist
Our priesthood celebrate
A diabolic eucharist
With chalices of hate.
Your system’s ripened fruits appear
In vampire and in sot;
The tiger women wait you here,
You soiled and left to rot.
See there a squeezed-out sponge of trade,
Or gambler’s child, or wife !
And there a haggard sempstress, speyed
By Competition’s knife 1
Within your walls anon there shines
A wrecker’s signal-light;
And falcon-featured Catilines
Sneak to and fro to-night.
Ah ! city dwellers, fearful wrong
Entails a fearful cost;
And ye who dare may see who throng
Those balefires of the Lost.
Melbourne.
DANDTON.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Neivspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
udiich such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 29, 1896.
The Beak and the Dead.
Victoria has longhad, and deserved, therepu-
tation of being one of the most snuffle-ridden
provinces of this continent. It is a land of
sour Sabbatarianism and aggressive Calvin-
ism, where the saints who have strong
orthodox views about the damnation of in-
fants are supreme. Its famous boom and
its wonderful burst are both monuments to
the holiness of Victoria, inasmuch as all its
worst frauds were engineered by the pillars
of the church, and almost all its prominent
men who ought to be in gaol, were, and still
are, notorious for their infuriated piety.
Victoria has more bitter orthodoxy to the
square inch than any other spot in these
provinces, South Australia alone excepted ;
and an element of snuffle and mediaeval piety
pervades not only its laws but the method of
their administration, and the pious J.P.
rages exceedingly in the land. Down in
Victoria they still occasionally bury the late
suicide secretly, and at night, with a vague
idea that it makes God feel better to see
the remains of the person who has died con-
trary to the views of the great associated
Podsnap hustled around with obloquy after
dark, and the Victorian J.P. probably
makes more severe remarks about the
suicide than all the rest of the Australian
magistracy put together. The latest case of
the kind turned up last week, and read as
follows :
During an inquiry into the suicide of the nonagenarian,
reported in Wednesday’s Age, the coroner, Mr. W. W.
Greene, P.M., expressed himself as strongly opposed to
the frequency with which juries attributed a suicide’s
action to unsound mind. He ordered that the body be
buried between 9 p.m. and 12 midnight, in accordance
with the powers given him by the Act.
» * * •
This sort of proceeding, of course, does no.,
harm to the deceased, and, so far as he is
concerned, it might go unnoticed. The spec-
tacle of a blatant magistrate jumping with
the large feet of his mind on a person who is
dead and therefore can’t defend himself, may
look foolish and indecent and in the worst
possible taste ; but as the person jumped
upon is probably beyond all knowledge of
the matter his share in the incident may be
passed over. The P.M. who has “strong
views ” about the conduct of the dead, and
orders his insensible dust to be hauled around
in a specially insulting fashion in a feeble
attempt to get level with him, is a natural
product of the law which authorises such
things, and the law is the natural product of
the spirit of public snuffle by which Victoria
is pervaded. In a certain low, degraded sense,
however, the Victorian procedure is more
logical than that of the other provinces. In
all the Australias the parson-ridden law re-
gards suicide as a crime, and when any citi-
zen attempts suicide and fails the law runs
him into gaol for a longer or shorter term to
bring him to a better frame of mind. If the
man wlfo only attempts suicide is a criminal
the man who actually achieves it would be
naturally regarded as a still worse offender,
and as he can’t be imprisoned for three
months, owing to very obvious and unpleasant
reasons, the only way in which the law can
express its opinion of him is to offer a special
insult to his remains. In New South Wales
and other provinces, the unsuccessful suicide
is imprisoned, but nothing happens to that
worse offender, the successful suicide, except
that he is allowed to remain dead. Vic-
toria, which is more logical in its illogicalness,
carries the proceeding to its natural termina-
tion by trying to follow him into another
world, and shakes an impotent fist at him
across the Styx.
* * * *
Some day it will probably occur to the Aus-
tralian nation that its entire attitude on the
suicide question is foolish and ridiculous, and
that the law dealing with the matter is an
anomaly, and that the administration of it
tends to make the bush J.P. even a greater
fool than he is by nature. The matter is
not in itself of any great importance, inas-
much as the old view of the question is
a relic of parsonical bigotry and intolerance,
and a scrap of the dreary superstition which
has kept the world more or less in chains for
centuries. There is absolutely no direct or
reasonably indirect scriptural condemnation
of suicide, and the Bible, which is supposed
to be the foundation of this country’s law
and morals, nowhere prohibits it. The gene-
ral feeling against it is partly a survival from
the old fighting-days, when it was every
man s duty to kill somebody else, and suicide
meant the loss to the tribe or nation of a
valuable individual who ought to have been
better employed in killing somebody else
before he died. It is partly a survival of
the days of slavery, when every man be-
longed to somebody else, and suicide meant
the destruction of valuable property ; conse-
quently, there being no other*way of influenc-
ing the suicide, the Church, as the friend and
agent and protector of the propertied classes,
announced that hell was the inevitable con-
sequence of self-destruction. Further, there
is a social prejudice against suicide because it
often tends to destroy the carpet, which is also
property ; and because it is sudden and in-
artistic, and uncomfortable for the people
who find the body, and it often throws the
expense of the funeral on persons who are
quite unprepared for it. Moreover, if there
is no hell for the suicide, then for whom is
there a hell ? And if there is no hell for
anybody, then what future is there for the
Church, and the collection-plate, and all the
rest of the sacerdotal establishment ?
* * * *
If suicide is any crime at all, then it is
murder ; there is no other heading in the
decalogue under which it can be included.
In that case, attempted suicide is attempted
murder, and the punishment should be 10 or
15 or 20 years' penal servitude, or there-
abouts. If the Church isn’t prepared to
demand that attempted suicide should be
treated in this fashion, then it gives away its
whole case. If the State isn’t prepared to
put attempted suicide on the same basis as
attempted murder, then it has no criminal
basis to put it on. The administration of
the law is fully as confused as the law itself.
If the suicide fails in his purpose it holds
that he was of sound mind, and fully
responsible for his actions, and it sends him
to gaol for seven days, or a month, or there-
abouts. It does this, either with the idea
that a man who isn’t afraid of hell will be
scared out of his evil purpose by gaol, or
because it thinks a person- who is too
miserable to care to live any longer will be
c l ie ? l . U P a ud made to take a brighter view
by a residence in a luxurious stone
building ; or because a month’s confinement
is its idea of a fair equivalent for attempted
murder ; or because it feels that it is a fool
in a cul-de-sac, and it has to do some-
thing, and can’t give a reason for doing
it. But it sticks with intensity to
the theory of the unsuccessful suicide’s
sanity, because otherwise it would have to
confine him in a lunatic-asylum for an in-
definite period, and it hasn’t sufficient faith
m itself to accept the responsibility. On
the other hand, when the suicide succeeds in
his purpose it almost always declares, with
or without evidence, that he is insane,
because its dismal orthodoxy alleges that the
suicide who is sane goes to perdition, and
the average bench hasn’t sufficient faith in
its orthodoxy or in its own perdition to make
such an assertion. Moreover, it is an un-
comfortable thing for any bench of gentle-
manly instincts to say about anybody, and it
might lead to retaliation from the deceased’s
big brother; so the average magistrate in-
structs the average jury to lie as politely as
possible about the late-lamented’s state of
imnd and the general decision seems to be
that it the deceased was sane enough to kill
limself scientifically then he was insane, and
if he was so insane that he didn’t know how
fished hmiSelf ’ then hIS san % is f ully estab-
To the credit, or discredit, of Victorian
law, it is much more given to facing this
difficulty than is the law of any other Aus-
tralian province. In Victoria it is a matter
of frequent occurrence for the suicide to be
planted secretly by night on the ground that
he killed himself with a full knowledge of
his sin, and is Lost, according to the good
old parsonical phrase. Then the back-blocks
J.P., who naturally knows all about it,
solemnly closes the doors of Paradise against
him, and orders his corpse to be treated with
disrespect according to law. By all the rules
and regulations of orthodoxy the deceased
has vanished into the infinite, and from
where he is the globe on which a small, un-
considered beak is making severe remarks
about his clay isn’t so much as a speck
against the background of the blackened
sky, and the matter affects him as little as if
somebody had a low of him in
Jupiter. The only excuse for the stupid in-
sult and the lugubrious midnight burial is
that it may hurt the feelings of the suicide’s
surviving relatives, and some other possible
suicide may possibly give up his purpose for
fear his midnight burial may hurt the feel-
ings of his relatives—in which case it seems
that the surviving relatives should be flogged
or beheaded in order to make the deterrent
as effective as possible. Or possibly there is
a shapeless idea in the mind of the illogical
beak that somewhere in eternity and infinity
the defunct may hear of what happened to
his remains, and feel aggravated in the
neighborhood of the Milky Way; or more
probably there is no idea at all in the magis-
trate’s vacant intellect, and he only acts as he
does for the same reason that a cow looks
over a gate in the afternoon. The law of
suicide and its administration are equally
tangled, and there is no sense or logic in
them.
* * * *
The shortest and simplest process is for
the code to cease the futile attempt to deal
with a subject which is beyond its reach. It
should give up trying to decide whether the
suicide was sane or insane, for the sufficient
reason that he is gone beyond its jurisdiction
and is never coming back—at least not in
any shape which it can recognise ; and his
state of mind is a matter which is not of the
slightest use to it, and to pry into the ques-
tion is mere'gratuitous impertinence. It
should also give up the foolish attempt to
wallop the dead, and to shake its fist across
the Styx at a ghost which may not be there,
and to throw an imbecile little brick at Pro-
vidence, by offering cheap insults to the
suicide s dust. It is illogical, for one thing ;
for another, it is the meanest kind of
cowardice to attempt injury to a person who
is down and will never get up again ; and it
is the worst kind of foolishness for the com-
mon J.P. to know more than the Scripture
on which Ins alleged religion is built. So far
as concerns the suicide who succeeds, all that
the law wants to find out is that he is quite
dead and that no person save himself is
lesponsible. As regards the suicide who
taifs, it has a right to repair him to the best
of its ability, on the off-chance that he may
.have changed his mind and may want to be
repaired ; and to charge him with the bill for
medical attendance, and hold him civilly and
criminally responsible for any injury he has
done, by bloodstains or otherwise, to any-
bodyeise s property—and there its respon-
sibility ends. It doesn’t know where the
successful suicide has gone, or where the
unsuccessful one was going to—even the
Church doesn t profess to know where the
place is situated that it says he was
going to. If there is any such place,
and he was going there, the place
m question doesn’t belong to the law, and
it nas no authority to warn anybody off the
grass. What the State knows about the
beyond is that it drops the convicted mur-
derer over the edge into it, and the depth is
so great that it has never yet heard the thud
o his arrival there ; and beyond that all it
knows is that it knows so little that it doesn’t
dare to teach the subject in its schools for
fear it doesn t know anything at all. And
this information is hardly sufficient to justify
it in stating definitely whether its citizens
should go there—wherever it may be—or not

and as the average policeman is capable of
thinking almost anything, there is a great deal
of interesting employment ahead for ihe force.
The people of uneasy virtue are having a really
fine time in Maoriland just now.
Divorce by Mutual Consent.
Ihe frequency and comprehensiveness of the-
plea of “ desertion ” in Hew South Wales,
divorce-suits tinge deeply the suspicion that,
“desertion” frequently means “collusion.”"
Which adds another degree of relevancy to the-
question—Why should not collusion be made-
an openly valid and tenable ground for granting;
a divorce? Why need perjury be added to-
collusion to enable unhappy couples to be-
legally sliced apart? Why should not the-
mutual agreement of any wedded pair
that their particular marriage has proved a.
failure be the best of all grounds for dis-
solving the partnership ? Any such a case is
not a matter affecting public interests. It is.
the wedded disaffecteds’ own affair. The
State has nothing whatever to do with
any religious ideas imported into the
marriage relationship ; in the eyes of the
law, that is merely a civil contract, and, like-
other contracts, can be mended or ended—
mended by a judicial separation ; ended by a.
divorce. If a religious faith impels one or
both of a married pair to hold binding views;
regarding matrimony, they are free to be boundl
and are not bound to be free. All that the-
State should consider is the desirability of
liberating fools who have fallen into their own
neis, and the necessity of seeing that the
children by a marriage are provided for. Man
and wife should possess the right to go before a
divorce Judge and say : “ See here ; we’re sick
of each other; neither of us wants to go and
commit any of the statutory offences of adul-
tery, continued drunkenness, felony, cruelty,
or desertion ; but one of us will have to do one
of those things if you don’t divorce us right
off.” And the Judge should be able to say :
“ Fees, please; or certificate of poverty. You
are divorced.” What more cogent reason can
there be for the cancellation of a civil contract
—and the law holds marriage as a “civil con-
tract ” only, which is why “registrars” are
endued with full splicing powers—than the
desire of each party to the contract that it
should be cancelled ?

The Kanakaland Bank.
Brisbane Worker is pegging away at the
thesis that the Q. N. Bank ought to be examined
and disinfected before the Govt, swallows it.
It has unearthed a variety of interesting infor-
mation concerning the bank’s transactions with
its political shareholders, and their transac-
tions with the public funds, and the continual
disappearance of the public funds in the re-
cesses of the bank ; and just now it is drawing
attention to a remarkable change (long ago
noted by The Bulletin) in the certificate given
by the bank’s auditors. Up to 1892 the Q.N.
Bank audit certificate read :
We hereby certify that the above is a true and correct
statement of the affairs of the Queensland National Bank,
Limited, on [such-and-such a date]. (Signed) Francis Q.
Southerden and W. Robertson Strong.
But at 30th June, ’92, the auditors x-eported :
We certify that, IN OUR OPINION, the above balance-
sheet is a full and fair one, exhibiting a true statement of
the affairs of the bank AS SHOWN BY THE BOOKS.
Nothing said of vouchers, nothing of examina-
tion of securities. Which, as The Bulletin
has previously pointed out, looks ominous.
And The Worker has ferreted out a few dozen,
other little circumstances of the kind, which go
to strengthen the strong case pre-existing for a.
survey of the bank’s affairs by a committee of
(1) the Q. Auditor-General, (2) a first-class:
Southern actuary, and (3) a skilled Southern,
accountant with a reputation to lose, before the
Govt, takes any more responsibility. Yet The
Worker acknowledges the hopelessness of the
struggle :
It would appear that the Government of to-day, being
apparently strong and allowed to have much their own.
way, another tin-pot investigation will take place unless
some newspaper does its duty.
The heads of Q. Labor-party, in fact, are be-
ginning to realise the utter impotence to which
their last-election policy reduced them. Had:
they chosen to climb down from their impossible-
platform, and unite with the Opposition to
catch the Moderate vote, they might possibly
have gained a Parliamentary majority, they
would almost certainly have gained a minority
only less than the majority. As it is, the score-
of Labor members in the new Parliament are;
practically only a row of ciphers. The Govt,
majority hopelessly outnumbers them, andi
votes them down consistently. They can talk,


but it all goes for nothing when the division-
bell rings. The result is that Q. Labor-party
will paks through this Parliament as it passed
through the last, without securing one reform,
one step in advance—nay, more, without being
able to hinder backward steps, without being
able to exercise the least influence on the
policy of the Government. As legislators,
the Queensland Labor-party may be counted
out. The Govt, does as it pleases. If
the Govt, wishes to consummate any job in
connection with the Q.N. Bank, no rectitude of
purpose, no array of arguments, will avail the
Labor-party one whit against the overwhelming
Govt, majority. Whether there will be any
honest examination of the Q.N. Bank’s con-
dition depends entirely on Premier’s Nelson’s
personal honesty and self-respect. If “ the
party ” can persuade his Scotch kirk-elder’s
conscience to whitewash the sepulchre, white-
washed it will be, and the Labor-party may go
and play marbles.
Italy and the Pope.
A problem that will complicate the choice of
the Pope has already found definite statement
in the European press, and peculiarly vivid
re-statement in the radical Italian papers —and
on this point nearly all Italian papers are
radical. Will the Italian Government allow
the perpetuation of Rome as the seat of the
Papal throne? The consideration has been
urged that no other Government of Europe has
ever been called upon to receive within its
boundaries, and to be compelled to tacitly re-
cognise the pretensions of, a sovereign who—
insidiously, it is true, but nevertheless inces-
santly—makes war upon its power ; and the
Italian Government is apparently determined
to force this issue upon the contemplation of
the world. As one Italian paper puts it, “ the
Vatican is the centre of veiled insurrection,”
and it is asking altogether too much from
secular human nature to expect that the Go-
vernment which is endangered, and whose very
existence, indeed, is challenged by the insur-
rectionary force, can longer tolerate the present
conditions. As no good Catholic, according
to the pronunciamentos of the Vatican, can
be a good and patriotic citizen of Italy,
it necessarily follows that the chief concern for
the Italian Government is to finally break the
Papal power, and, therefore, the chances are
that it will only agree to the next Pope having
his home in the Vatican on condition that the
Papacy shall abandon once and for all time its
itemporal claims. In other words, the Italian
ipeople will demand the impossible, for the
assertion of those claims will never be aban-
doned by the Church, and because compliance
■with her impossible demands cannot, in the
.nature of the case, be made, then there will be
nothing for it but for the new Pontiff' to find a
twentieth-century Avignon. For the world,
ihowever, there is the supreme consideration
that the Papacy will never voluntarily abandon
Rome. Rome is not merely a figure and type
for “the Mother of all Churches.” The city is
;an integral element of the Church’s splendour
and her seeming unity. Break the tremendous
itie which connects the Church to Rome the
‘City, and you sever the historic, the ecclesias-
tical, and the sentimental bonds which bind the
infallible Church of to-day to the irrevocable
.past —to the continuity of her traditions, and
.to the ever-full reservoirs of her faith. When
the Papacy departs from Rome, the dogma of
Infallibility will have to be repealed.
The Co-Respondent that Should Have
Been.
Vic. Chief Justice Madden’s recent famous
■divorce decision seems, at time of writing, almost
certain to be overturned, like many of his other
■decisions, by the Full Court. Mr. Madden
■decreed that a husband whose wife had gone
•wrong could not get relief because he had
■allowed her to follow her profession as an
■actress, and travel with a theatrical co., and by
thus exposing her to temptation had forfeited
all claim to redress. Evidently Mr. Madden
didn’t look where he was going when he took
this amazing jump. He implied either of two
things. One was that the theatrical profession
is so immoral that every actress is expected to
go astray unless her husband has his eye upon
her all the time, therefore any man not in the
profession who marries an actress, or any man
in the profession who marries her but who can’t
get a constant engagement in the same co.,
must take it for granted that his wife
will go wrong. And as no actor can get
a constant engagement with his wife—
the Dacres tried it and the experiment
ended in ruin and poverty and a tragedy
at the memory of which the public still
shudders this means that every actress is
expected to go astray unless kept constantly
chained up. And his action in refusing to biff
the co-respondent with the strong arm of the
law means that any actor has a right to lead
her astray without suffering the usual conse-
quences ; the actress is excused because she is
led astray by the seductive heavy villain, and
the heavy villain is excused on general prin-
ciples. The other possible reading of Mr.
Madden’s judgment is that evert/ man who lets
his wife out of his sight is expe<sted to take the
consequences, and that the consequences may
be expected at any moment, and that Mr.
Madden thinks it is only the natural thing for
every woman to go wrong if the old man isn’t
alwaysathome, or if he goesout without firstlo.ck-
ing the lady up in the strong room. In one case
the whole theatrical profession, male and female,
has good cause to heave rocks at the hair-waxed
C.J., and in the other the whole female popula-
tion, and all the male population that has any
respect for the female population, has reason to
throw road-metal at him. Also, it is not quite
clear that Mr. Madden, by his own logic, isn’t
an indirect co-respondent and wrecker of happy
homes by inference. His Honor, in his pre-
judicial days, was a patron of boxing-matches
where men bashed each other’s heads for fame
and a cash consideration. Many of these men
were married. When they came—partly at
Mr. Madden’s instigation—to bash and maul
each, other they had to leave their wives at
home, which was practically the same thing as
if they had allowed their wives to go out and
leave them at home ; and they thus—partly at
Mr. Madden’s instigation —exposed these
ladies to serious temptation. When Madden,
C. assumes a lofty immoral attitude concern-
ing men who part from their wives in the course
of business, he ought to remember the large
number of men whom he has incited, more or less,
to part from their wives in pursuit of the ear-
chewing profession, and the possible conse-
quences of that separation, and assume a
humbler tone in consequence. Also, The Bulle-
tin regards the C.J. as a direct incentive to
immorality in another sense. By his own
theory, a man who lets his wife associate with
the seductive tragedian shoves her along the
downward road by exposing her to temptation.
But when a husband habitually comes home—
partly at Mr. MADDEN’sinstigation—witlihiseye
blackened, and his nose knocked endways, and
his ear six times its natural size, and his jaw on
one side, he also exposes his wife to temptation ;
the sight of that awful object—for the wreckage
and disfigurement of which Madden, C.J., paid
away his good money—is enough to make any
woman elope with anybody merely to get away
fromit. What is the difference between leading a
woman away by exposing her to the seductions
of a handsomer man than her husband, and
driving her away by making her husband uglier
than any other man? Will Chief Justice
Madden answer?
The Gospel of Greenstuff.
“1 have used no animal food for 40 years,”
boasted an ancient Sydney vegetariariatic at
the recent annual cabbage-worry of the fad,
“ and therefore I am well and hearty at 82 ! ”
This is the -post hoe with a vengeance, and even
at its best the argument cuts impartially both
ways. If A, who is vegetabilious, is entitled
to attribute to that fact his survival of B, who
wasn’t—then C, who isn't, is equally justified
in crediting to this circumstance his survival of
D, who was. Also, there is absolutely nothing
to show that, other things being equal, the
average Nebuchadnezzarite doesn’t die sooner
than the average omnivore, or get into gaol
oftener, or that he isn’t more liable to marry a
woman with one eye. Also, again—how does
this indubious ancient know that he could ever
have got through 40 years of chastened cow-
diet if it hadn’t been for the constitution built
upon his preceding 40 of unregenerate beef- and
mutton ?—or can even any life-long green-feeder
be sure that he hasn’t hung on to existence
mainly by virtue of the stamina inherited from
centuries of more or less carnivorous ancestors ?
Also, once more—what does this whole Gospel
of Garden-stuff make of the plain fact tiiat
before the flesh-consuming peoples the rice,
maize and banana-eaters have gone uniformly
to the wall ?—or of the unavoidable conclusion
that, if Nature hadn’t intended Man-animal to
subsist partly upon flesh, she would have fixed
him up—dentally, assimilatively and digestively
- —upon some such plan as the Ox-animal. When
the Vegetarian Society has successfully achieved
the chewing of its cud, its kail-yard cult will
have a leg to stand on ; meantime the butcher
has no cause to tremble !

The Australasian thinks, or says it thinks,
that the Leg. Council should have control of
the public finances, and wants some reform
which will enlarge its authority in this direc-
tion
When Mr. Duffy, at the Mayor’s quarterly dinner, in-
terjected “Never, never” to Sir Frederick Sargood’s
prediction that ere long; the Leg;. Council would be given
some voice in finance, the company no doubt understood
him to be joking, as usual. The people who pay the bulk
of the taxation—certainly all the direct taxation —are not
likely to consent to any important changes in the suffrage
unless extra strength is given to the only House which
represents them. We are rapidly arriving at a state of
things in which one set of people impose taxes and vote
away the money, while another set has nothing to do but
pay such taxes. An Assembly elected by adult suffrage
would have for its constituents an immense body of voters
who pay scarcely any Customs duties and no direct taxa-
tion. The temptation to squander the revenue would,
under the new circumstances, be irresistible.
This is interesting, as showing what the House
of 48 Rich Landlords means to do with Turner’s
One-Adult-One-Vote Bill when it comes along.
The property-owners of Victoria have now' a
larger say in the Assembly than the non-pro-
pertied classes by virtue of their plural vote.
Also, they have entire control of the Leg.
Council, so that the Fat Man is fully repre-
sented in two Houses and the Lean Man has a
small voice in only one. The statement that
the Council is the only House which represents
Fat is a fine piece of lying; the Council is the
only House which represents Fat alone, but
that is quite a different thing, and why the Fat
Man should have a House all to himself while
the Lean Man hasn’t passes comprehension.
The Australasian’s definition of wdiat the
present Upper House wants is a solid reason
why the Sargood crowff which goes up for re-
election next month should be ejected bodily.
If Victoria misses the opportunity to throw
that mob out on its ear, then it fully deserves
whatever may happen to it. What will happen
so far as the Adult Suffrage scheme is concerned
seems tolerably evident. The price proposed
to be charged for that measure is the surrender
to the Council of an equal share in the control
of the finances, and even adult suffrage would
be six times too dear at such a figure. It would
only mean that every citizen would have an
equal vote in the election of the Assembly ;
but, as a contra, the Assembly would be so
much shorn of its authority that it would be
hardly worth anybody’s while to have any vote
there at all. The Vic. Fat Man has more than
an equal voice on financial matters now by
reason of his plural vote in Assembly elections,
and his demand for another equal voice all to
himself is a very large order.
If one-half the allegations made up to date re
the management of the Melbourne post, tele-
graph, and telephone office are true, that
department should be about the most wonderful
sink of corruption on this continent. ' So far it
would seem to have existed mainly for the pur-
pose of supplying jobs to one favored contractor
at extraordinary prices. One of the charges
alleged against him is £4 14s. for repairing a
coal barrow, the value of the work being esti-
mated at 17s. 6d. He is also accused of receiv-
ing £ll Bs. for stores worth £1 18s. ; and for
13 jobs, of an estimated value of £32, he is
alleged to have sent in a bill for £lO3 and been
paid quite cheerfully and without any demur.
It is further averred that some of the goods
never came to hand at all, and that some of
them were wholly useless ; but as a book, which
was supposed to throw a lot of light on the
matter, has disappeared, and no one knows
where it is, and the most wild and infuriated
search can’t bring it to light, the inquiry is at
present going on three legs and making less
progress than it might do. The board, which
was long busy hunting up evidence about fes-
tive ladies who called in to see officers of the
department and did casual high-kicks and other
exercise, has now got into dead earnest, and
seems likely to break something.
The Peacock Factories Act (Vic.) does not
specifically provide for a weekly closing of
shops for half-a-day—but indirectly that will
be one of its general effects, beginning in Oct.
next. Shopkeepers halt between two opinions.
Wednesday? Saturday? And all the while
employees pray that the choice may fall on
Saturday, so that “ the evening and the morn-
ing ” may be the holiday. The larger city shops
close on Saturday afternoon already—and this
privilege cannot be taken from them even by a
majority. Many suburbs now celebrate a
Wednesday half-holiday—reserving Saturday
till 10 p.m. for the big business of the week.
To have the “universal Saturday ” would be a
great boon to thousands now kept working
while all the world enjoys itself; but, of course,
Wednesday, if generally recognised, would
become a sports day, and would make a
pleasant break in the week’s work. One of the
surprises of the situation has been to find so
many shopkeepers willing to discuss closing on
Saturday, usually worth three other days for
business, when there is a prospect that other
shops would do the same, and that “ wages
day ” would be shifted back to Friday or
Thursday.
Nothing but male loyalty and public pressure
will keep women from playing havoc with cur-
rent male wages-rates in the new branches of
industry to which Australian Governments and
other employers have recently admitted them.
Some of the late Postal appointments of women
are said to be accompanied by a scale of pay
pointing to decline. Men, however strongly
opposed to the employment of women, must
now, seeing woman has forced admittance,
stand by her in demanding payment at man’s
rates, or men’s and women’s wages will both go
down (as in Maoriland, where two compositors,
husband and wife, work for wages which in the
aggregate do not amount to more than a man’s
pay according to the Australian standard). If
men don’t support women in claiming men’s
wages, employers, State and private, will re-
duce men to women’s rates. In Lancashire
(Eng.) all weavers are paid alike. Men and
women work side by side, and the pay-sheet
takes no stock of sex. There the men stood by
the women, and wages now scale on the male
level. In the textile industries of Yorkshire,
male workers stood aside and the male wages
are every month sinking nearer to the female
level. When work is- equal, sex can’t count as
an extra. The water-level is what the em-
ployer can get the work at.
Dear Bulletin, —How is the Argus weakness
for giving local militia potentates their full
military titles on all occasions to be reconciled
with the strenuous efforts of the conservative
print to be caustic at the expense of the Ameri-
can colonel, captain, general, judge, etc. ?
What is true and impressive dignity in the
Britisher is howlingly absurd in the American
citizen. So dignity, like morality, has its geo-
graphical limitations, and, as with true mora-
lity, so with unquestionable dignity, it is made
only in Britain and her dependencies, and not
for exportation. No wonder the Vic. Tory
press bolsters the militia ; it is a veritable hot-
house for the forcing of young Tories. The
militiaman who looks for advancement becomes
a blatant Imperialist, and naturally an ossified
Tory ; all his ideals are at ’Ome, and his
ambitions, and usefulness, and beauty are only
appreciated to the utmost by the conservative
papers that are always shrieking for more
defences, and picturing the poor private Aus-
tralian citizen crouching terrified behind a noble
wall of soldiers who defy his foes for him at so
much a month. The foes are purely imaginary,
and nothing more is needed. “ Defiance, not
defence,” should be the motto of the Victorian
militia. —S.
“ All religions that I fell in with in Ireland seemed to
me too irreligious ; really, in sad truth, doing mischief to
the people in place of good.”— Thomas Carlyle. “ I can-
not help thinking that religion is the root of Irish misery.”

Wasthe late Monarch William aourse or ablessing?
It isn’t a question which troubles us much ;
For this is the point we consider distressing—
The bone of your Irish contention was Dutch.
Disputes on the topic of Saxon oppression
Spring up from the sod of your Emerald Isle,
But the passions provoked by an Orange procession
Are simply and truly religiously vile ;
So, rush into action, supporters of faction,
Who love to distraction
A pious belief:
Go, scatter and shatter the brothers you
batter,
And finish tne matter
By coming to grief.
“ Equality under the law ” is the coming, and
now near, cry of the Australian So-
licitor Kong Sing very properly told Stipendiary
Deloiiery, in a Sydney Court some few days
ago, that he did not see why Chinamen were
raided and hauled to justice and heavily fined
for playing fan-tan or pak-a-pu, while Euro-
peans who gamble in pubs, and clubs and pri-
vate houses remain unmolested. Chinamen in
North Queensland, remembering the rights
possessed by the squatters of old, petition the
Government to shift the straggling aborigines
from their neighborhood, or to, in effeet, allow
them to use the squatter-plan of “dispersal. In
other parts the same appreciation of the posi-
tion and the same demand for the advantages
and privileges of the white man are taking
substance and shape:—and where is the human
reason that can rebut the cry ?
Q. Chief-Justice Griffith has presented to
Parliament some “ Notes on Australian Federa-
tion,” in which he says, with appalling circum-
locution,, that on the colored-labor question at
the present time a federal authority couldn t
possibly know where it are. Griffith’s extra-
ordinary mind pulls so many different ways
that it will never be made up in this world, and
doubtfully in the next. No other Australian —
not even’ Parkes —has left such a serpentine
political track. At various times, Griffith
has opposed Chinese immigration ; has opposed
coolie ditto ; has indirectly supported Chinese
until his political opponents nicknamed him Ah
Sam ; has declared kanaka laborers to be
worse oppressed than the victims read of in
“ the blackest annals of the slave-trade has
encouraged and legalised kanakas ; and has de-
spatched an emissary at public expense to
Europe to arrange for the importation of “ mean
whites.” Griffith, while not pretending to be
particularly pious, has had the political support
of the Churches (probably because his pa was a
Congregational parson) ; he has had the support
of the teetotallers, while consistently drinking
whisky ; has been the leader of the “ great Q.
Liberal-party ” without being a Liberal; has
had the adoration of the Labor-party—and its
detestation; was leader of a Bar that profes-
sionally and socially “ cut ” barristers who be-
came “ legal practitioners ” (“ amalgams ”), yet
was the first Q. Premier to make a “solicitor-
only ” (Mein) a Supreme Court Judge. Was
there ever such a political polyhedron ?
Victorian law on the subject of treasure-
trove seems a trifle mixed. The other day
a Melbourne man was acquitted at the Supreme
Court on the charge of larceny of and receiving
a watch and chain which his little daughter
had found and his wife had pawned. The
Crown said, by its prosecutor, Walsh, Q.C.,
that “if a person found an article he was ex-
pected to make reasonable effort to discover the
owner.” The Crown said by its defender —
well, the Crown didn’t have a defender, so his
view is unobtainable—and Judge Holroyd
summed up that “ if the accused at the time he
received the watch had no intention of appro-
priating it, he was not guilty of larceny.”
But not very long ago Madden, C.J., said that
any person finding an article in the street was
entitled to keep it; and more recently Judge
Williams declared the exact opposite, and said
that such person was not entitled to keep it,
even though no claim were made for it. What
he was to do with such article the Judge
omitted to say. Perhwps he didn’t know. In
the face of all this, how does the State itself
stand ? If an article be left in a railroad car-
riage it is passed on to the lost-property depart-
ment. The State contents itself with advertis-
ing a monthly or quarterly list of its findings in
the Govt. Gazette ; and should the owmer
(possibly under the impression that it was lost
elsewhere) advertise for its return, the State
takes no notice whatever. Yet, at the end of
six or twelve months the State sells all these
lost articles by auction and collars the proceeds,
thereby showing its intention of appropriating
the lost article or the value thereof. Things
are mixed.
A Public Health Bill which Maoriland Parlia-
ment is now struggling with provides, among
other things, that every ship-master arriving in
M.L. must make a declaration that none of his
passengers are consumptive, and failing this he
will be fined £5O ; if he lands any consumptive
passenger he will be fined £lO and the consump-
tive will be fined a similar amount; if he allow's
a consumptive to occupy the same cabin as
another passenger he will be fined <£so ; and if
a person proves, within three months of his
arrival in M.L., to be consumptive, he shall be
deemed to have been consumptive on arrival,
and the captain who brought him shall be held
responsible. This last clause will make matters
tolerably interesting for the captain, who is
required to diagnose consumption three months
before it becomes generally visible. A glance
at the death-statistics, however, seem to cast
some doubt on the logic of the whole business.
In 1894 there were 576 deaths from consump-
tion in Maoriland, and 287 of the patients were
born in the province, while there were only 41
out of the 576 who had lived in it for less than
five years. From this it would seem that M.L.
mostly grows its own consumptive, and that the
tuberculous cow -and pig in the province are in
a great deal more need of inspection than the
tuberculous man who arrives from outside.
The chief reason why political debates on Pro-
tectionism versus Freetrade, or so-called public
orationson either of these questions, are generally
unprofitable and beside the mark, and useless
as demonstrations of any idea or ideal, is that
each advocate merely “ begs the question” and
fabricates his premises. The Protectionist
w'ould-be light is too busily engaged in slang-
wlianging the iniquities of the importing
monopolist to build up any logical edifice in
support of his cause ; and the Freetrade apostle
is too recklessly eager to demolish the manu-
facturing monopolist to pay any attention
to any other aspect of his case than “ the curse
of cheapness. Wanted : A real live debate,
in which it shall be taken for granted at the
outset that both importers and manufacturers
will, whenever they get the chance, grab all
they can, fine or tax the working-man all they
can, fatten all they can, and give as little in
return as they can—after the ordinary fashion
of ordinary human nature. In short: A debate
on Protectionism with human greed as its
basis v. Freetrade with human greed as its
basis. After which, the application of the
issue to the particular people or country inter-
ested should supply the solution of the vexed
enigma. There is no absolutism of any fiscal
theory. There is no fixed gospel. To a com-

The

t Dominion
munity marooned on a barren rock in mid-
ocean, protectionism would be a hideous
handicap. To a people living in an island-
continent, fertile and resourceful, but threat-
ened by greedy foreign naval powers, Freetrade
might mean extinction or subjugation or
starvation. The diet or physic of a babe
would be innutrient and weakening to an
adult; the diet or physic of an adult would
eufever a babe. Also, a babe must crawl, or
be aided to walk, before it can walk of its own
volition and without bandying its legs. Why
not apply the simplest rules of common-sense
to fiscal surroundings, instead of talking rhodo-
niontade about millennial theories ? Given the
straight-out issue : Fat Man v. Lean Man, or
Haves v. Have-nots, which fiscal theory is more
profitably applicable to the majority (i.e., the
Have-nots) in Australia ?

Atiienasum Hall Rev. Chas. Clarke.
The combination bill, “ Cavalleria llusti-
cana ” and “ David Garrick,” which filled in three
nights at Sydney Lyceum, after Tosca had finally
bumped herself to death over the Roman battle-
ments, did something to improve the reputation
of the Potter-Bellew combination. In “Caval-
leria,” Mrs. Potter was the same exaggerated,
wild-eyed, wildly-painted, posturing creature as
reported last week, but in the more peaceful
character of Violet Gresham she gave herself a
fair chance with very pleasing results. Her great
beauty—which was wholly lost in the beplastered
countenances of Tosca and Santuzza—asserted it-
self, and her costumes were a dazzle of glorious
millinery. She put comparatively little force into
the part ; when she ordered the inebriated Gar-
rick out of the house it was done with the even,
placid voice of one who asks for change of a half-
penny, and she fled from her hard parent in an
emotionless monotone which was wholly unequal
to the situation. But in all the beauty scenes of the
play Mrs. Potter was a great and undeniable suc-
cess. . Bellew’s Garrick was effective at every point,
but in “Cavalleria,” Scot Inglis, as Alfio, the
teamster, overshadowed everybody else, and made
the one unquestionable success of the little tragedy.
There was more picturesqueness and more life and
energy and realism about him than in all the rest
of the show, and when he polished-off Bellew,
after getting his ear considerably bitten, and
Bellew had died a rather unreal kind of death, he
was the only person that the audience cared to re-
member to any great extent.
* * * »
The P. B. co., at time of writing, is doing
a spell of “ As You Like It ” to excellent business,
and Mrs. Potter is about the prettiest and best-
dressed Rjsalind that Sydney has yet seen. There
is a tendency to exaggeration about her in places,
and she is somewhat unreal in other places, and in
some more places she almost forgets to be artificial
and is in danger for a moment of being a really
charming and clever heroine, and the general
impression is—at least, it is just going to be, when
the actress degenerates into her old artificialism,
and then the audience really doesn’t know what its
impression was, or was going to be. Still, Mrs.
Potter’s beauty and her occasional touches of
brilliance, or something that might be brilliance if
it didn’t depart so suddenly that the audience
hasn’t time to be quite sure what it might have
been if it had stayed long enough to be anything
in particular, carry her through, and the general
effect is good. The co. is an unusually strong one ;
there is not a weak spot in the performance,
barring the bewildering Potter, concerning whom
the public hasn’t begun to be. certain whether she
is a weak spot or not. Bellew’s Orlando is a
strong, consistent performance. Scot Inglis is
perhaps the best Jacques who has pervaded the
Australian stage, and Miss Polly Emery the best
Audrey, and the veteran Cathcart fills the part of
the ancient Adam for all that patriarch is worth.
The show is mounted in a large, rich, expensive
fashion, especially for a six-night performance,
and the next thing that happens is “ Camille,” on
Saturday, for three nights only, after which
“ School for Scandal” fills a similar period.
* * * *
The “ King of Crime ” pursues his course
of battle, murder, and sudden death at Her
Majesty’s until Friday, and on Saturday he will
give place to “True as Steel.” There is not so
much unexpected funeral in this piece as in the
present production, but it is remembered in
Sydney as a healthy and solid drama with a moral
as large as an elephant, and a reasonable amount
of corpse. Marschner, trick cyclist, has been
temporarily on the programme at H.M.’s, and
some of his doings are calculated to make even the
stolid Plugger Martin green with envy.
* * * *
Maggie Moore abandons the character of
the mesmerised maiden with no stockings on for
the last three nights (Friday, Saturday, and Mon-
day) of her season at Sydney Royal, and farewells
in good old “Struck Oil” and the “Chinese
Question.” Friday is Miss Moore’s benefit, and,
as Premier Reid and the rest of the God-fearing
Ministry are to be present and the surplus will be
on view in the vestibule, it promises to be a very
large event indeed.
** * *
Harry Rickards has done nothing more
than usually extraordinary at Sydney Tivoli this
week, Crackles and the Frantz Family and the
mysterious Provo being still in possession. La
Bell Maie has returned to walk her airy wire, and
some other people are expected, and sundry more
people are going away shortly. The Ovide Musin
Concert Co., under Lohr’s management, is due at
the Centenary Hall on Saturday night, and the
return of Bland Holt from a place called Brisbane,
which is beside a creek somewhere, is beginning
to be remotely expected.
** * *
A ghostly exhibition, said to be inspired
by Rontgen’s discovery, is going just now in King-
street, Sydney. A man, fully dressed, is shown
standing in a coffin. The lights are lowered and
then raised again, and the occupant of the coffin
is a skeleton. In response to requests from the
audience the bony fragment waves his arm and
gnashes his forbidding jaw. Voice from the
audience asks him to “ Have a beer,” but this is
obviously absurd as he has no place to put it, and
the memento treats the suggestion with silent
contempt. Then the lights flicker down again for
a second or two, and the bony one is gone and the
man is back again, and the person who made the
rash offer of beer goes surreptitiously away.
** # *
“ The Nominee,” in which Nat Goodwin
finishes his Melbourne season to-night (Thursday),
is one more story of a comic married man going
forth upon the razzle-dazzle and suffering subse-
quent tribulation at the hands of his mother-in-
law. Years ago, Harry St. Maur introduced
“ The Candidate ” to Australia, with some suc-
cess. “ The Nominee ” is a bright American
variation of “ The Candidate,” and a much less
impossible joke; Nat Goodwin is a better
comedian than St. Maur, and altogether the sh«w
under notice calls for a general expression of re-
gret that it didn’t come along a fortnight earlier
to prevent other things from happening mean-
while. There is a political backbone to the
narrative. The razzling sinner gets returned
to Congress on the wrong ticket unbeknown
to himself, for whilst he should have been preach-
ing the Jacksonian doctrine of Freetrade to a
savage tribe of Protectionists in a remote elector-
ate he was otherwise engaged with a gaudy Annie
at New York. And when Annie tracks him to
his domestic circle it appears that she really came
to capture somebody else, four male members of
the cast having given their hearts to this dissolute
female at one time or other. The feeblest of the
four victims ends the disturbance by volunteering
to marry Annie, to whom a wedding, more or less,
will make very little difference.
* * » *
Nat Goodwin is a fine, broad, polished
joke in the name part of this amusing piece,
whilst the company—barring only the feeble
youth aforesaid —supports him well enough. The
Misses Elliott—Gertrude and Maxine —made their
first appearance oil Saturday night, and proved to
be valuable additions. Miss Gertrude is a neat
little actress : Miss Marine walks majestically
through the character of the razzler’s doting wife,
and excites great astonishment at his conduct in
leaving her for fourteen days. Most husbands
would think it advisable to be tied to her apron-
string henceforth and forever.
“ Romeo’s First Love,” preceding “ The
Nominee,” might easily have been an unwarrant-
able insult to Divine William, but the author (one
A. E. Lancaster) deserves credit for an earnest
little effort to depict an incident in Romeo’s career
prior to his meeting with Juliet. The damsel in
this case is uninteresting, and some of the Shake-
spearian characters chant their lines on the panto-
mime-fairy principle, nevertheless the ambitious
trifle, elegantly staged, gives Miss Blanche Walsh
a chance to create a graceful young Romeo. Her
share of the performance was a decided success on
Saturday night, and this journal takes the oppor-
tunity to heave a sigh over the rumor of Miss
Walsh’s immediate return to the land of her birth.
* * * *
“ Romany Rye, ’ at Melb. Royal, promises
to restore some of the lost business to that vener-
able establishment. The public that stayed away
from “Cheer, Boys, Cheer ” for unknown reasons
is disposed to make amends by rolling-up to the
older melodrama, and the overthrow of the mur-
derous individual who stole George Rignold’s
birthright, or purchased the papers belonging to
his girl, is received with much acclamation.
“Romany Rye ” comes of a tough race of melo-
dramas. During ten years’ absence from Mel-
bourne it has lost none of its early vigor. The
chief villain curses his gipsy relative as heartily
as before, the inebriate bird - fancier is still
on the original burst, and the Mother Shipton
who sits brooding over her evil past in a damp
cellar seems rather younger than the creaking hag
of long ago. Likewise, the shipwreck has kept
pace with modern improvements. “Romany
Rye depends a good deal upon its famous marine
disaster, and the present rendering of the foain-
fleeked waves is all that could be desired, if not
more so. George Rignold, Plimmer, Sweeney,
Miss Watts Phillips, Walter Raynham, Miss
Williamson, and Miss Emily Hughes (as Gertie
Heckett) have most to do with the plot. Generally
speaking, they do it rather better than it deserves,
but not so well as Brunton did the scenery.
“ Romany Rye ” seems likely to run another week,
although “ Called Back ”is ready and anxious to
take the floor.

Revd. Charles Clark will breathe his last
lecture at Melb. Athenaeum Hall on, or about,
Saturday (29th), and.Musin will give a farewell
matinee at the same spot this afternoon (Thurs-
day) previous to leaving for Sydney.
* * *■ *
On Saturday “ A Trip to Chinatown ” will
be produced at Melb. Princess’s, an announce-
ment which is quite superfluous under the cir-
cumstances. The city has been talking of the
American burlesque for some time, and everybody
knows that the hour of his arrival is at hand. On
Friday the Princess’s closes for preparations.
** * *
A very ’igh-life performance of “ The
Parvenu ” is to come off at Cliveden, the Mel-
bourne residence of baronet Clarke, on Sept. 1, in
aid of the local Fever Hospital. Pretty Miss
Mary Clarke is cast for leading lady’s part, and
her brother Frank will impersonate the pampered
flunkey in attendance. Lord Shaftesbury, Lieut.
Col. Bingham, Mrs. Alfred Cornish (related to
colonial royalty), also a Mr. Vivian, whose name
sounds promisingly proud and haughty, will be
principal male conspirators in this deserving out-
rage, and the price of tickets for the good cause is
fixed at 7s. 6d. Three half-crowns is a reasonable
figure for an entertainment of such social magni-
tude, therefore Lady Clarke exhorts patrons of
the charity drama to book their seats early. Chil-
dren in arms and other disreputable characters
will not be admitted.
** * *
Hearing Musin and Scharf play the
Kreutzer Sonata to a moderate audience at Melb.
Athenaeum, one felt a hearty contempt for the
local “musical world” whish had swarmed into
the Town Hall, a while back, to enthuse over
the vastly inferior performance of Kruse and De
Beaupuis. Verily the Kruse boom was the greatest
of R. S. Smythe’s achievements in gammoning
the public.

There is medical authority for suggesting
that the Nerveless Man on tour may possibly bean
addition to the roll of lepers. Dr. Sam. Williams,
of Melbourne, states that the N.M. case reads
exactly like one of “anaesthetic leprosy” (little
known to the faculty in Australia), which doesn’t
show itself on the skin. Dr. Williams has sampled
this interesting disease in ’Murka, or somewhere,
and stuck pins into it without giving the patient
any discomfort.
