The Australasian Securities Investment Corpo-
ration, whose prospectus appears in another column, is a
quaint idea in the way of subdivision of labor. The
Association proposes to raise a capital of £250,000, though
there is power to increase it up to £2,500,000. This will
be invested in the shares or debentures or other securities
of joint-stock companies, not more than 10 per cent, of
the capital at the utmost being* fixed in any one security
though it is intended to keep the limit much lower than
even this, the idea is that the Association, being run by
cute business-men and its risks distributed over a large
area and carefully watched, will always more than balance
any losses through the depreciation of one security by the
rise of another, whereas the individual investor may have
his all m one investment and see it go hopelessly broke
The idea is sound enough and quite feasible, and the pro-
posed Association is under excellent management \ still
there is something grotesque about it. Nowadays men
form companies to do almost everything. Next they begin
to form companies for the express purpose of holding
shares in those companies. And, if the securities invest-
ment corporations become numerous, someone may yet
strike the bright idea of forming a co. for the express
purpose of holding shares in them. And as every co. in-
volves some kind of a manager and office and board of
directors, and as the possibilities in the way of forming
co’s to hold shares in other co’s are practically unlimited,
it follows—well, it is difficult to say what does not follow.
* * * *
That struggling institution, the Australian De-
posit and Mortgage Bank, has closed the half-year with a
profit of £69—equal to about fourpence per cent, per
annum on capital. Even this is a great improvement on
the previous three half-years, which showed losses of
£2616, £7566, and £5631 respectively. The recent reduc-
tion in interest, however, saves about ' 9000 for the half-
year, therefore the dilapidated institution ought to have
been £9OOO better than it was for the half-year ended
June, ’95, and as it is only £7635 better it would seem
that it is really— apart from the saving in interest—£l36s
worse. Still, in one respect it shows an improvement:
deposits have been reduced by t'72,000, and now stand at
only £594,000. This is almost Ihe only' bright spot in the
report. Interest on loans and investments (£10,944) is
£776 less than last half and is less than £1 14s. per cent,
per annum on the investments, and if the co.’s money is
lent out at an average rate of only 5 per cent, this means
that fully £BOO,OOO of the alleged assets are unremunera-
tive, or dead, or more or less lost, or comatose, or some-
thing. Freehold property (£66,000) yields £367 (not
much over 1 per cent.), which is too humble to be worth
mentioning. The A. D. and M. Bank may have got round
the corner; it is something to have made any kind of a
profit by any process whatever, and the fact that its pro-
perties have begun to be saleable once more is encourag-
ing ; but it has a long, dusty road still before it.
* * » »
The Land Mortgage Bank of Victoria, another
casual survivor of the boom, announces that the net loss
is £9696, compared with £BS47 and £6616 in the two pre-
vious six-monthly periods. The Land Mortgage has only
£250,000 of paid-up capital, and owes £900,000 on deben-
tures, so it is woefully top-heavy. The debentures bear
V P er cent, interest, and a callous gang of English inves-
tors have so far refused to make a reduction, but
further negotiations are in progress. A reduction of
interest to 2 per cent, would just save the concern, if
things don’t get any worse, but, otherwise, its prospects
look about hopeless. A call of £1 per share (£250,000)
has been made, but it would require a call of nearly a
million to pay off the ruinous debenture-holders and put
the concern on its feet, and as the proceeds of the call up to
30th June were only £5lBl, the progress so far is hardly
worth mentioning. Seeing that the L.M. Bank is trading
almost entirely on borrowed money, and the said money
costs twice as much as it earns after paying working ex-
penses, and it is hardly possible to get a large enough re-
duction to make ends meet, and things are apparently
getting worse rather than better, so that even a reduction
to 2 per cent, might only postpone the trouble, and con-
sidering that the shareholders’ little capital is probably
more than all gone in depreciation already, and even on
paper the assets are £40,000 less than the liabilities to
shareholders and the public, and no progress worth
speaking of seems to be made with the realisation of the
assets, it looks as if the best course for the Land Mort-
gage Bank would be to give up the ghost—what ghost
there is.
* * * *
The Standard Bank (Melb.), still another weary
fragment of the boom, announces that it has lost £7063
for the half-year, as against £1513 and £4908 lost for the
two previous half-years. Also, £224,107 has been written
off for bad debts, and a depressing appendix states that
further losses will have to be provided for. The de-
posits have been reduced by £42,000, but they still
amount to £682,000, which is a stupendous load to
shift. Calls are £66,000 in arrear, and seem very
hard to collect. Even on book - values the assets
are now £240,000 less than the total liabilities, which
means that nearly half the capital has disappeared over
the horizon. The gross takings are less than 2) per cent,
per annum on the written-down value of the securities.
The Standard some time ago struck an arrangement with
its depositors, whereby they agreed to take 2J4 per cent,
interest in cash and the remaining 2 per cent, in new
deposits due 1903, but as the institution is not earning,
over and above expenses, even the 2| immediately pay-
able, it is evident that, unless things improve, the bottom
must fall out of it sooner or later.
* * *
The Commercial Bank of Tasmania shows a
profit for the half-year of £4024, as compared with £3261
for the last half and £4410 the one before. A 5 per cent,
dividend and income-tax absorb £3766, so there is only
£258 to carry forward. The earnings are equal to rather
less than 3 per cent, on the shareholders’ funds, so things
are none too gorgeous with the old Commercial.
* * * *
United States banking law defines a bad debt as
one the interest on which is more than six months in
arrear and which is not covered by full security and the
security in process of realisation. Any debts with interest
over six months in arrear and which are unsecured, or
where the security is not being realised, are not allowed
to be reckoned as assets, and if there are not sufficient
other assets to cover liabilities a Govt, official shuts up
the concern. The U.S. finds that State control is quite
possible, though the Australian Fat Man professes to re
gard it as an anarchical idea which has never been tried—
a mere impossible, impracticable vision of wild-eyed men
with long hair and bombs in their hands. The system
was adopted in the States by hard, conservative men as a
mere matter of business, and the whirling dynamiter is
attached to it here in a perfectly irrelevant fashion to
scare off the public, though he has no more to do with it
than a massacre has with a P/N at three months.

An irreplaceable store of medical instinct
and experience goes to the grave with Dr. Thomas
Chambers, who died at Sydney of pneumonia on
Sunday last, aged 07. Dr. Chambers was probably
Australasia’s leading gynecologist, and was, before
he came to Australia for his health, a dozen years
ago, one of London’s best-known “ woman-
doctors.” To the last, he was an ardent student.
He shone equally in diagnosis and in operative
surgery, and had the complete confidence alike of
the medical profession and of hosts of patients.

M Cracken’s Brewery (Melb.) is appealing to its
debenture-holders (mostly English) to reduce interest from
4£ to 3 per cent., and extend the date of repayments
from 1908 to 1916. The institution is loaded down with
£500,000 of debentures, so the reduction would save it
£7500 a-year. As the loss last year was £22,923, while for
1894 it was £9983, and for 1893 there was a loss of £4636,
it is difficult to see how a mere saving of £7500 a-year can
do much to keep the institution on its legs. The co. has
a paid-up capital of £500,000, and, as already mentioned,
owes £500,000 on debentures. Also, it has a growing
overdraft; its assets consist mostly of £900,000 worth of
hotels and other properties at cost, and the said assets are
even then admittedly £53,000 less than the liabilities
to shareholders and creditors. And what is the present
value of £900,000 worth of hotels, &c. ? Would they fetch
anything like enough to cover the debenture-holders
alone?

Victoria Coffee Palace Co. (Melb.) reports a
profit for the half-year of £674 (equal to about 3j per cent,
per annum on capital), against £479 last half ; and, with
a steady increase in business, a gradual diminution in the
overdraft, a reduced rent, and a lifting of the depression,
dividends begin to appear possible once more.
* * » *
One great point to be noted in tbe Police-court
hearing of the City of Melbourne Bank case was the treat-
ment of an all-important witness, Edmund Rouse, by
both the Crown and the defence. Mr. Rouse’s examina-
tion and cross-examination was expected to last for many
days. He is the ex-manager of the London branch, who,
in'September, 1892, telegraphed to Melbourne enquiring
whether the bank was involved in B. J. Fink’s insolvency
and to what extent. The reply was a ghastly tarradiddle,
understating the amount of Fink’s liability and. overstat-
ing that the bank was fully covered. Rouse’s testimony
to this vital fact in the Crown case was elicited in the
most unobtrusive manner possible. It wasn’t enlarged
upon, or held up to public notice by the examining coun-
sel, and within the short space of twenty minutes the
gentleman from London was bustled out of the witness-
box. There was no cross-examination whatever. Alfred
Deakin managed to interject an assertion that Rouse was
“trying to prejudice the case,” but having fired his blank
cartridge, the mock-indignant barrister took care not to
attempt to prove where the alleged “ prejudice ” came in.
Chief witness Rouse had stated a damning truth against
the City of Melbourne Bank management, and seemingly
the general policy of the enquiry was to give a gentle
cough and look as though nothing unpleasant had
occurred.
* * * *
The City of Melbourne Bank crowd had one
anxious moment when Rouse was giving evidence
as to certain figures. Barrister Deakin interrupted
Rouse, who promptly flared up and threatened
mischief—“ Don’t put words into my mouth. Are
you giving evidence here or am I? Will you hold
your tongue until I ask you a question and don’t
make assertions concerning what you know nothing
about. Don't interrupt me or I will .” The witness
stopped short, and presently apologised for having lost
his temper. But the threat was truly awful whilst it
lasted.
* * * *

It would be as interesting as Kaye’s letters ’Ome re the
Mercantile Bank trial.

G. D. Meudell is trying to get Melb. Stock Ex-
change to abolish the fees charged for the quotation of
new mining co’s. Stock Exchanges generally consist of
very conservative persons who can’t, or pretend not to,
see the absurdity of exacting fees to enable them to earn
commissions. They soon break through the rule when
good co’s decline to pay the fees—as in the case of
Charters Towers mines. Most of the money lost in share-
gambling filters into brokers’ pockets. «

A Chinese marine insurance co., the Yangtsze
Insurance Association, has started business in Sydney.
Another development of the frantic competition in fire
and marine biz. in Australia.
* * * *
The Melb. building societies are just beginning
to slaughter wooden houses. They have held on for
years, but conclude that it is no longer any use, and the
house-knacker is in full bloom with bodily removals. To
say that houses are sold at 80 or 90 per cent, discount on
what the luckless victims paid is too mild. The proverb
about fools building houses and wise men living in them
is here illustrated.
* * » *
Bricks are literally dirt-cheap in Melb., and
capitalists are building a good many houses. The stagna-
tion is only in building society mullock, and the depres-
sion will about clear away the wooden part of Melb.

* * * *
Melbourne Full Court, having granted uncon-
ditional certificates to Matthew Davies, J. B. Davies, and
other insolvent boomsters, was bound to upset Judge
Molesworth’s ruling in the case of C. B. Fisher, the good
old squatting identity who failed for a million and a-half.
Judge Molesvvorth found that the insolvent’s schedule
was a record of reckless speculations, and in a plain-
English acceptation of the term “ reckless ” this finding
was undoubtedly correct. The Full Court got over the
difficulty by stating that Mr. Fisher’s schedule revealed
nothing more than “ a prudent and generous man might
have done.” It was not the business of any captious logi-
cian in a horse-hair wig to denounce the Full Court’s law.
C. B. Fisher’s certif. had no bitter opponents, and it came
in the natural course of things Melbournian. But he
might at least have been spared the charge of prudence
in conjunction with due credit for generosity. Better to
have held him up to public respect as an insolvent whose
recklessness was free from the popular taint of dishonesty.

Sydney Mint received in ’95 less N.S.W. gold
than in ’94 to the extent of 20,6710 z. —a similar shortage
of Q. gold amounting to 108,5050 z. —which Deputy
Master Macdonald Cameron explains in his report to
London as arising from stoppage of mining operations
through want of water. The official estimates, however,
show that the Q. yield did not drop to nearly the extent
suggested by Mr. Cameron, and that the N.S.W. yield
actually showed an increase of 35,0000 z. The fact appears
to be that gold is diverted from the Sydney Mint by the
very high coinage charges that for some reason are con-
stantly maintained there. Sydney charges are for certain
parcels three times heavier than 'Melbourne charges.
Much N.S.W. gold is annually exported unminted, but
practically every ounce of Vic. gold gets coined.

Mount Rae, Clarence district, N.S.W., is being
worked by an English syndicate. Tunnelling, so far, has
opened up about 1500 tons of good stone, showing gold all
through. The completion of a new tunnel now started at
the bottom of the hill will, when completed, mean about
5000 tons of gold-bearing stone ready for breaking; reef
18in., country diorite. A battery is under order.

Rainfall at Broken Hill for first seven months of
year, 3%in,
Pambula : “ Two Melb. gentlemen representing
considerable capital have gone out to Jingera to inspect
and report on mining properties, and it is to be hoped
that some of the properties that are now languishing for
the want of capital will change hands.” It never seems
to strike the ordinary mining correspondent that capital
is languishing for the want of mines. Yet, solemnly
speaking, that is the true position of affairs throughout
Australia. Because of the millions of idle cash available,
mines in Australia are bringing a bigger price than in any
other part of the world. Money is plentiful and so are
“ wild-cats,” but good mines are scarce, and of these few
are for sale.

» * » *
So far, there being no sign of general condemna-
tion of W.A. “ wild-cats ” in the Argus articles on Western
mining, by Mr. Herbert Daly, an ex-officer of Vic. Mines
Dept., the boomers are in great fettle. One, who signs
himself “ Victorian,” gives himself away by congratulating
the paper and the writer on “simply stating things as
they exist in an accurate and clear manner, and not
launching out into impossible comparisons, or his opinion
of the estimated dividend value of the mines.” He is also
quite sure that “ the general public have a very vague
idea of the little stoping done to produce the splendid
crushing results already recorded.” “ Still harping on the
Boulder group ! What about the Great Boulder ? ” is
thrown at the present writer 50 times a week. Well, if a
group of good mines, all in one comparatively small area,
is sufficient for 1000 wild-cats, on which millions of money
have been and are being wasted, then the writer will retire
from the mining arena. At present, he is inclined to think
that (without imputing any want of honesty to Mr. Daly)
the public will vote for Briggs, who had nothing to gain
by telling palpable, though unpleasant, truths.
* * * *
Cosgrove’s mine, Coolgardie—what has become
of it? What is the battery doing? The public either
hears a great deal or nothing of any mine of this name.
At present it hears nothing. Forfeited shares are adver-
tised for sale, but that is not satisfying. What, too, has
become of the last rich strike in Bayley’s ? The last re-
port sayeth nothing : “ Reef 4ft. wide in Everard shaft,
no free gold.” Where, oh ! where are the Plunderderry
and the Wealth of Expectations? And in looking down
the list of English W.A. companies not floated this year
but early last, and some the year before, the same ques-
tion might be asked of 30 other mines.
•** > *
Engineers who have studied the details of the
W.A. Govt.’s proposed water-supply for the Coolgardie
fields are generally of opinion that it will involve more
than double the estimated cost and take five to six years
to carry out. This is good news for a lot of mines which,
without water, can manage to live without gold. Long
before the scheme is carried through, a railway will run
to Esperance, the present opposition of the Forrest Govt,
notwithstanding.

Norseman : “ The Queen of the West has struck
a sensational find. Seven pounds weight of pure gold
was broken yesterday close to the surface, some of the
lumps of gold being as big as a man’s fist.” One lump
should therefore weigh more than 71b. What is the weight
of the Norseman whisky?
Again from Norseman : “ The Mt. Henry
mine is creating excitement and the country is pegged for
miles. (Good old phrase !) Capt. Prout has visited the
ground and he says that there is enough payable stone in
sight on one block to keep up an output of 1000 tons a
day for 20 years.” So this one block has only 7,300,000
tons in sight—just about enough to keep a 1000-head mill
running for 100 years. And this is the sort of stuff the big
Australian dailies print as mining news !
* * » *
Dr. Simon on his return to Westralia : “ It can-
not be denied that investors in London, Paris, and Ger-
many are growing anxious for returns, and, unless the
mines begin to show their value, confidence will ulti-
mately wane.” The wonder is it has'not waned long ago.
It is difficult for mines to show value without gold. The
want-of-water-and-machinery wheeze is nearly played out.
How long did the Great Boulder take to show its value
after it struck the new formation from which the whole
of its gold has come? How quickly it was followed by
the Lake View and Ivanhoe, as soon as they got on to
similar formations ! Why are there more idle batteries
within 10 miles of Coolgardie, in proportion to the number
erected, than in any other part of Australia ?
• * * *
The shareholders of Block 45 at Hannan’s have
decided to reconstruct, taking 160,000 paid-up shares and
giving away 70,000 shares to find a working capital of
£25,000. Perhaps a fine thing for the future of the mine,
but for the immediate present it is a much better thing
for Messrs. Wilson and Horn, who will probably have no
difficulty in disposing of the shares at par, thereby netting
£35,000 by the deal. When this has happened it will
suddenly occur to some of the shareholders that the old
co. might have done the same thing itself and made its
own profit. Then the co. will proceed to kick itself.

W.A, newspaper hummers are not even satisfied
to leave well alone as per average sample. “The Lake
View mine is raising stone from the 200 ft. level worth Boz.
and 9oz. to the ton, and assays from the hanging wall of
the lode at the 200 ft. level are going 30oz. and 40oz. to the
ton.” Of what use is such stuff in the face of the many
hundreds of tons going through the battery for an average
of about 3oz. ?
» * * »
Specimens bringing <£lloo from the Queen of
the Earth mine, W.A., sold by auction in Coolgardie, and
2cwt. more sent to London. A year ago, for any mine to
submit specimens to auction would be regarded as con-
clusive evidence that the owners were not up to their
business. Madness now consists of sending the 2ewt. to
London. Since the almost immediate collapse of Bayley’s,
the Wealth of Expectations and the Plunderderry, after
each had exhibited rich specimens in London, J. Bullion
gives the W.A. specimen-mine a wide berth, and the men
responsible for any more exhibitions will probably meet
with a violent death. Golden rule—never buy into a
specimen-mine. “‘ My face is my fortune, sir,’ she said.”
* * * *
As a neat example of some of the Westra-
lian alleged crushing-returns, last year a man from
London was sent to a 90-Mile lease to watch a crushing
of over 100 tons in the interests of intending English pur-
chasers, the vendors being a very influential crowd. The
result was a trifle over 3dwts. per ton, the tailings con-
taining about 2dwt. more. Hie property was declined
with thanks, but subsequently a syndicate took it up, on
the recommendation of a man who said it averaged 2oz.
to the ton. It was floated in London, and another crush-
ing took place with similar results. The reconnnender
then put on his own men, and Western papers now gravely
state that a 2oz. crushing has taken place.
* * * *
Bendigo and Coolgardie manager, lamenting the
barrenness of the half-year’s work, thus stimulates
scrippers : “ We are in no worse position than a great
many other W.A. mines of some value—in fact, we are in
a far better one than some of theni—and with much
better prospects.” On this comparison, what sort of a
gordelpus show must the Bendigo people be hanging to ?
* * * *
All the representatives of Bull’s money don’t
buy mines in the Coolgardie country, and of these nothing
is said. One much-travelled personage, who has been in
the possession of a full outfit—camels, cooks, type-writer,
and such things—covered 5000 miles, examined over 400
alleged gold-mines, and was so disgusted generally with
the mines, the prices asked, and the conditions of mining
in the country, that he did not take any.
* * * *
Another mining manager writes: “ Last year
a lease at North Niagara was sold in London on the
strength of reports by two ‘experts.’ It was placed
under my management, and I proved beyond the shadow
of a doubt the reef did not go as many dwts. as it was
represented oz. ; mine was soon ignominiously shut down.
I enclose extract from Perth paper to show how somebody
is trying to explain away a very awkward situation.”—
N.R.'H.
* * * *
Briggs, the Age and D.T. special commissioner,
is letting some daylight into over-boomed W.A. mines.
Assays of a few inches of rich part of the lode have been,
by the aid of newspapers, applied to the whole width—9ft.
Both papers are printing indictments against their wire-
correspond ents.
* * * *
Charters Towers divs. for July, says Bright’s
Circular, reached £16,877 6s. Bd.; calls for the month,
£8525. Divs. for the seven months, £139,860 Is. lid.;
calls for same period, £69,901 6s. 6d., leaving a balance to
profit of £69,958 15s. sd. The July crushings were the
best for several months—2o,726 tons for 22,6300 z
there were three fortnightly clean-ups in the Brilliant and
St. George. Without this, however, the yield was over
20,000oz. for the month, which total is now regarded as
the field’s normal yield.
* * • *
Two sugar-mills erected under the Q. Guarantee
Act were in operation last year, and both easily met
liabilities to the Treasury. Ten mills will be in operation
this season with a total output of 40,500 tons, making the
charge to the Treasury for both interest and redemption
£40,000, or £1 per ton.
* * * *
Mt. Morgan divs. for the last 10 years amount
altogether to £4,200,000, or £4 2s. per share. Ore treated
totalled 591,643 tons with an average of a little over 2oz.
per ton. Total yield, l,470,486oz., worth about £4 an
ounce. Wherefore sum-total of gold turned out is about
£6,o00,000 sterling. And the co. assert it has another
£6,000,000 in reserve underground.
* * * *
Papuan Brilliant and Victoria, Charters Towers,
600 tons, 1269oz. Charters Towers mines never looked
better, the market never worse. Speculation has fled to
Westralia. Investment as safe as most things on and
under the earth can now be made there with a certainty
of 15 per cent, by buying into, say, half-a-dozen div.-
paying mines. The writer has tried it, and, therefore,
knows a little of what he is talking about.

The latest joke on Melbourne ’Change relates to
the interviewing of A. J. Miller by a verdant representa-
tive of the Age. A. J. Miller, the “ mining expert from
the Transvaal,” who has come over to represent “a power-
ful English association in working an extensive and pro-
mising reef at Omeo,” is generally understood to be the
ex-Melbourne “sport” who started the powerful associa-
tion aforesaid in London. And the working of this reef
by the Mount Hepburn Co. figured on the prospectus as
part of a general scheme for the development of the coun-
try and the promotion of immigration. Nothing about
these huge national undertakings appears in the Age
interview.
* * * *
New Red, White and Blue Consolidated ranks
as Bendigo’s strongest div. mine, in six years having cut
up £119,000 amongst seven stockholders—principal, reefer
Lansell. Past-half’s figures : Milled 12,810 tons for
4607oz. 8dwt., £5625 divs. Cost of working slightly under
£1 a ton. Stone in sight assures a healthy future.
Hustler’s reef waking up again. Last fortnight’s
112 tons for 40oz. is an improvement on the previous
yield, 153 tons for 25oz. 9dwt. The old mine just
manages to pay its way.
Johnson’s reefs fortnight’s stamping, 413 tons
for 109oz. Idwt. Still in low-grade country, but the pro-
spects of better stone at the deeper level are cheering.
Loss of £3172 on the six months’ work.
Probable that the Eureka Extended, to avoid a
very costly 800ft. sink to get at the New Chum reef, will
tribute its deep ground to the New Chum Railway. Means
tacking on a good lump to the latter’s deep div. life pro-
viding the reef keeps up its average strength.
Lady Barkly, with 25oz. 9dwt. from 325 tons, is
looking dicky for calls. At Bendigo it is either a feast or
a famine. Directors never build a reserve for future pro-
specting or keep the development work well ahead, but
simply work the gold-shute for all it is worth.
Pearl’s half-year’s crushing, 1761 loads for
543oz. 14dwt. Scraped through with one div. and £35 to
credit. A new shaft is rapidly getting down to 500ft. to
test the northern ground, where the co. thinks it has a
• large slice of the Red, White and Blue reef to work. A
successful strike there would put a heap of heart into the
Pearl.
Lazarus dawdling till the shaft gets well over
the 3000ft. mark, where crosscuts will be driven to test the
reef struck higher up. Stone broken during the bi-term
averaged a shade over 6dwt., which, however, didn’t save
scrippers from a call.

* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—Re Vic. Mt. Morgan. I must
admit the various reports hereon are conflicting and mis-
leading, but, all the same, the discovery is a valuable one.
Govt. Geologist Murray reported that the lode runs from
N.W. to S.E., but I am inclined to think there is no lode,
properly speaking—that the whole mountain ridge is a
mass of the peculiar formation found in the prospecting
leases. It is a sort of sandstone conglomerate, inter-
persed with quartz veins and pyrites, containing a little
gold, and, according to recent assays, a fair amount of
silver. There are numerous outcrops in all directions, so
it is impossible to say where is the beginning or end of it.
At the junction of two creeks about two miles above the
mine the fall is great and it is possible that enough water
exists to work a battery in winter, but I much question
if they will be able to dispense with steam in summer.
I don’t think the rock can be treated by ordinary crushing
process alone: it will require chlorination or cyanide. I
have taken up three leases and intend to work them for all
they are worth.—E.H., Moonee Ponds.

CAMEO
The Bulletin hears that at a meeting of
Oonfucians last month in Kucheng (China) it was
resolved to thank the “Australian Missionary So-
ciety for its continued supplies of lady-mission-
aries, and to ask it to send more Australee gel
by every steamer. It was stated that Confucian
males, who are studying the “modern religion
called Christianity,” are only just beginning to
appreciate the Australee gel, and that the demand
for her far exceeds the supply. It was pointed
out that the “ modern religion ” was one founded
on “love,” and that was a point at which Con-
fucians could meet young and plump lady-mission-
aries manfully.
AT A BACKBLOCKS ACCOMMODATION-HOUSE.
DAUGHTER OF ALL WORK (to traveller): “Will yer give me the
brush I gave yer to brush yer ’air; I leant to do some
scrubbing.”
TRAVELLER : “ Certainly ; and could you get me a blacking-brush
to give my boots a rub?”.
DAUGHTER OF ALL WORK : “ Yer can use this brush for them, too,
and I’ll ’ave it nicely washed by the next time yer want to
brush yer ’air.”
At the other end of this question : It is reported
that a deputation of Sydney Chinese waited upon
the Mission authorities, the other day, to protest
against the continued export to China of lady-
missionaries, of whom there were not enough to go
round among. Sydney applicants. The spokesman
was Want Kow, who said: “ Follin tlade welly
goo’. Too muchee follin tlade no goo’. Too
muchee sendee gel China. Allee nicee fat gel
teachee Chinaman go way. No goo’. Me natche-
lisee Blitishee ; me wantee goo’ gel teachee me
Klistanitee ; Wing Tart, he wantee gel; Hung
Hell, he too; Ah Kiss, Ah Woo, Ah Buk, and
evlybodee allee same. Wot fo’ sendee allee nicee
fat gel to China ? ”
Yet cynics ask: “What shall we do with our
girls?”
* * * *
Apropos of the China - mission women’s
craze. Why don’t they look round their own
doors, where so much is to be done ? enquires a
Bulletin correspondent. Far-off fields are green,
and the “heroines” and “martyrs” leave home
to —travel at the expense of a fanatical public, and
lord it over the “poor benighted heathen.” The
country districts of Australasia offer a wide field
for work—yet helping hands are often withheld by
those who profess much. A recent Tas. case in
point: A half-witted woman with two encum-
brances, not blessed by Church-union, lost by
death her “ protector ” shortly before the advent
of a third child. Totally destitute, she had to
apply for Govt, relief to keep body and soul
together. The “ good ” folk would not help be-
cause she had led an immoral life, i.e., was not
“ married proper. ” Alone she bore her travail in
a wretched hovel. Two or three days after her
confinement she found herself without provi-
sions, and had to walk five miles to the town-
ship to procure some, carrying her infant
with her. She got a small bag of flour,
&c., and struggled back with her burdens.
The trooper, making his rounds a few days later,
looked her up and found her dying. Immediate
steps were taken to send her to a city hospital,
which necessitated 80 miles’ journey by coach and
rail, but she “ passed out ” before she reached
there. What a brutal satire on the yellow-heathen
mania ! If all men are brothers, all women are
not sisters, or cousins either; and, though charity
begins at home, there is more glory if one gives to
help those who have no direct claim on us—who
are not of our kin, but make use of what we send
them, and scoff at us in return.
The daughter of a well-
known N.S.W. squatter has
eloped and married a groom
employed on one of her father’s

shows indications of taking a
sensible view of the situation
and helping the young couple
to make the best of this
life.

Dear Bulletin, — Be the
lack of Celtic bards—every in-
telligent observer knows that
the Irish mind is unpoetical.
Irishmen are always glib, fre-
quently eloquent, talkers, with
a turn for blarney, but their
intellectual gold is mostly
coarse, very coarse. National
poverty may be solely to blame
for their vulgar sen tin it. The
more refined Irish of lad future
will breed poets. In the mean-
time, they must be content with
breeding barristers and politi-
cians.—Saxon.

rvice Board is, in its treatment
of teachers, considerably ahead
of the Bananaland Govt.,” re-
marks a Bulletin correspond-
ent. “Some time ago the Nor-
thern teachers (dead against
the intention of Parliament)
were severely retrenched by
the Boodlewraith, many of
them losing from 10 to 20 p.c.
of their salaries. It was openly
said in Parliament that the
Educational System was re-
sponsible for strikes and social
discords . . hence the ne-
cessity for a gradual lopping-
away of the evil tree. The
average salary in the North is
below that in N.S.W., while
the cost of living is much
higher. . . . In the face of
all this the head officials of
the Northern Education De-
partment write wailing reports
about the difficulty of getting
young men of superior intel-
lectual attainments to enter
and remain in the service—a
service which has been assidu-
ously packed with cheap female
teachers (whose only study is
the great book of Matrimonial
Prospects) and underpaid pu-
pil-teacher boy-labor.”
In re a curious judicial decision on a
divorce application recently, a correspondent asks:
“ Ought a. Judge who is supposed to be, and who
sets himself up as, a devout R.C., or who is a
member of the Anglican Diocesan Synod, to be
allowed to adjudicate on divorce matters, seeing
the decided views held by both the churches
named against divorce; it being assumed that
Judges are sincere adherents of the creeds they
profess to bolster ? ” The weakness of the question
lies _ chiefly in the assumption in its tail. The
ermine is supposed to blot out religion, always
excepting the traditional little soupgon in “ May
the Lord have mercy on your soul ! ” when a
gory sacrifice is being ordered.

KINDLY SEND FOR A CIRCULAR.
A sidelight on divorce. Respondent’s
solicitor lately intimated to a N.S. W. petitioner
that he would offer no serious objection to the
husband’s suit, provided the latter claimed £lOOO
damages against co-respondent, and settled the
money on respondent.
* * * *
While defending an “ under-16 ” seduc-
tion-case in Melb. Criminal Court the other day
David Gaunson quoted the late Dr. Hearn’s
“ Aryan Household ” to the effect that if an Act
were passed which was not in accord with popular
sentiment juries refused to be bound by the evi-
dence, and thus put a check on inconvenient legis-
lation. This appeal to the jury to repeal the law
was unchecked by the Judge. Counsel also
brought a volume of Romeo and Juliet into court
to prove the unnaturalness of the law as it now
stands. He characterised the Act as a direct in-
centive to crime, and claimed that the time was
come when boys should be protected against girls.
The girl in this case had admitted that she told
accused she was nearly seventeen.
* * * *
Apropos the Age-of-Consent question, once
more Sydney’s moral epidemic. With some, it is
deadly earnest crankiness ; with others, a wallow-
ing in upside-down vice and diseased voluptuous-
ness. The subtlety of its attractiveness is that its
disciples can mouth morality and parade maxims
while talking absolute filth all the time. Consider
a possible test of the virtue of the Social Gim-
leteers: How many parsons among them would
refuse to marry their own, or a friend’s, 16-year-
old daughter with a millionaire? Is there any
Female Apostle, now ready to sew the skirts
round .the feet of the Age of Consent, who
wouldn’t give her 16-year-old daughter or niece to
immediate wifehood with a millionaire of any age?
Again, is there in all the tents and tabernacles of
One-eyed Gimletdom one Age-of-Consent Male
Elder who wouldn’t jump at a chance—or implore
his favored son—to marry a 16-year-old mil-
lionairess ?

“ F.” to The Bulletin :
Re “B.B.” and that “Pleasant Sunday Afternoon.”
Much of the barracking of the white-collar brigade is
towards raising the age of consent. This may be laud-
able ; but why for females only ? Why this old-time dis-
crimination of sex, this squint-eyed view of things, not as
they are here now, but as they may have been at some
other time in some other climate ? To listen to the Rev.
Hegoats one would imagine the colonial population to be
divided between male wolves and feminine lambs ; as a
AFTER THE FRAZZLE."
“ Brace up, old man, I'll have to be off or my wife
won’t speak to me when I get home.”
“ Lucky dog. Mine —hic! —’ll talk to me all night."
matter of fact, ( the saddle is on the other horse. It is safe
to affirm that in at least half the cases our females seek
their own seduction. Is not the poor, callow youth",
preyed on by the designing beldame worthy of consider-
ation ? There are a score of Mrs. Potiphars to one Joseph
Surface, and, in bush courting, in nearly every case, the
active partner is the woman. Everywhere you will see
faded spinsters married to lads under-age, unsophisticated
youths trapped by the lures of old campaigners. And what
worries our Australian mother most is her boys —she
knows what the girls are capable of. And, grauting that
in morals Parliamentary interference is desirable, it
should go all round. “An age of consent act for girls”
would only be justified by a corresponding “ close time
for boys.”

“ Co-respondent—the right man in the
wrong place.”
Railway - Commis-
sioner Mathieson has
decided not to adopt
the patent door-closing
apparatus on the Vic.
railways. Probably he
is wise. An apparatus
which closes all the
doors simultaneously
before the train starts
and opens them all at
once when it stops
would make an im-
mense saving in wages,
but it is doubtful if it
would pay all the same.
When each door is
closed separately by an
official, he generally
sees that no one has his
head or feet or fingers
in the way before he
bangs it; besides, the
people see him coming
and take their heads
and the other parts of
them out of the road.
A machine which didn’t
come round and give
notice, but just banged
all the doors at once by
an invisible power,
would brain or maim or
scalp somebody several
times a week, even
though it gave notice
that it was going to
bang the doors by blow-
ing a whistle that rent
the heavens. In fact,
for the first seven years
or so, till people got
quite used to it, the
whistle would make
some people put their
heads in the road for
mere curiosity. Then
all these people would
sue the Government for
=£lo,ooo damages, and
the average jury, which
is always agin the
Guv’ment, would give
them big verdicts. And
even though 40 officials
swore that the whistle
was blown unto 70
times seven, it probably
wouldn’t make any difference. Unless some one
can invent a door where nobody can get his head
in the way the apparatus has its drawbacks.
* * * *
Cardinal Moran, in proposing a grand
Australian Fair as another step towards the com-
pletion of St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney, referred
to the Holy Church festival at Melb. Exhibition
which realised =€13,000 profit, and invited Arch-
bishop Carr to disclose the secret of that glorious
loot. There wasn’t much secret about the working
of the Catholic Fair in Melbourne. The prettiest
young female members of the persuasion were_
fixed up behind stalls and “race-games,” and
utilised in every line of cash-extraction, and all
the Murphys, Dalys, Dooleys, O’Sullivans, &c.,
received official instructions to roll up and spend
their money somehow. A good few thousand
photos, of Archbishop Carr were sold on the pre-
mises at prices from a shilling upwards, and a de-
votional trade in crucifixes leavened the lump of
gambling transactions. The Fair was just a great,
giddy bazaar, with a spice of religious sentiment
dropped in at intervals. It started with Arch-
bishop Carr’s blessing, and ended in a sort of
spiritual Irish jig, and in those cases where any-
thing less. satisfactory than =£13,000 came of the
blessed shivoo the sinners confessed their little in-
discretion and got rather more absolution than
usual.

* * * *
Latest developments of the eternal Ade-
laide Hospital-trouble are that the local doctors
are accused of endeavoring to establish a system
of espionage on the action of the new Hospital-
doctors within the institution, and that Prof.
Watson, of the University, who accepted the post
of honorary consulting surgeon to. the Hospital,
has received a letter from the local branch of the
British Medical Association asking him to appear
before it and explain. This he has pasted up
in the dissecting-room at the Hospital as a sort of
“memorial brass.” i

* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent writes from
Kempsey, N.S.W. :
On Saturday evening, just before dark, a man with a
heavy swag tramped up the main street of Kempsey. A
few steps behind him came a woman, apparently his wife,
pushing in front of her a large box fixed on to the under-
carriage of a four-wheeled perambulator, and loaded with
odds and ends of luggage. Behind the woman came a
girl of about 12 years carrying in her arms a fair-sized
infant; and-bringing up -the-rear of the sad procession
was another youngster who plodded along with its hands
wrapped for warmth in the folds of its thin pinafore.
They were “ on the wallaby,” and had moved close on 12
miles that day; youngsters and all.

continuing the Attorney-Generalship as a political
office.

* * * *
Little Chief-Justice Way, of S.A., boiled
over the other day because a prisoner whom he
had sent up for 18 months wished him a merry
Xmas. A Victorian bench also boiled over re-
cently because a lady whom it had sentenced to
three months expressed a hope that it would be all
dead by Xmas. The bench, taking it all round, is
rather hard to please.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

A Mother’s Appeal.
Thk following letter, dated 12th July, 1896 from
Mrs. E. J. Grien, St. Kilda, Newton, N.S.W.,
appeals to all parents : “My little boy, now aged
14 years, suffered with dropsy, which extended 111
over his body. He could not lie nor walk about,
he was such a terrible size—even his head was
so dropsical that it was twice its natural size. He
was quite blind, and his breathing was very
labored. I took him to a number of the cleverest
medical men of Sydney, but they could do nothing,
and I began to think that my poor boy was near
to his last days. One day, when passing a
chemist’s window, I saw ‘ Warner’s Safe Cure for
dropsy.’ I purchased one bottle, and, when
finished, the swelling seemed less, and his appetite
and digestion better. His digestion had been so
weak he could not take a drop of milk.
‘ ‘ I was so delighted with the change, I sent for
more Safe Cure, and now I can say this medi-
cine saved my dear boy’s life, and trust that this
may be seen by other parents who have dear chil-
dren suffering. I would like them to see what
Warner s ‘ Safe ’ Cure has done for my son.” #
T be (it Gal Life Conjpaoy
Z. 0. RENNIE, General Manager for Australasia.

At Poverty Point .
A travelling Australian, who went to see the first night
of “ My Girl ” at London Gaiety, tells Tiie Bulletin that
the lady, instead of being flippant and free as of yore, is
another clergyman’s daughter, domestic, demure, saint-
like, and insipid, in long skirts of virgin white that never
rise above her ankle. The curtain rises on Charles Ilvley,
who, by way of earthly reward for his surpassing piety
and innumerable acts of ascetic virtue, has become Vicar
of Barum-Stoke. Nothing is left of the Charlie of old
“Gondolier” times save the rolling eye, the extended
arm, and the cuddle. He is sad, solemn, cadaverous,
while the white choker and upturned optics invest him
with an even greater holiness than we of Australia were
accustomed to impute to him. Like all stage parsons, the
Rev. Ryley has a daughter, “ My Girl,” which her name is
Ellaline Terriss, who comes in for the cuddle. The plot
of “ My Girl ” can be bought in any Family Herald sup-
plement. Ethel Haydon, who is in the piece, plays and
sings charmingly. Grace Palotta appears in an unbecom-
ing frock and has two speaking lines, and Florence Lloyd
is in a pretty frock and has no speaking lines at all.
A well advertised Melb. singing teacher has a talent for
discovering wonderful possibilities in the voices of most
women who come to her for an opinion. She is as popu-
lar as the ladies-doctor who advises the patient to seek a
long change of scene. “John, dear,” or “Dear papa,”
the aspirant cries, “ I’ve consulted madame, and she says
I ought to go ’Ome at once for a course of training.”
Dr. Neild’s supper-party in honor of Emelie Melville
was a gay affair, by all accounts. One guest declares that
17 toasts were properly honored, but his recollections of
the festival from the time of the eighteenth speech are
vague and unreliable.
Sydney organist Wiegand, interviewed by an Adelaide
reporter : “ ‘ You have played several pieces by Australian
composers. What do you think of these compositions?’
M. Wiegand replied to this query with such a very ex-
pressive pantomime gesture that the scribe thought it
best not to pursue the subject further”!
Judge A’Beckett opined thus in the Richardson divorce
appeal last week (Richardson had been refused a divorce
by Madden, 0.J., on the ground that he had exposed his
wife, an actress, to temptation by allowing her to join and
travel with a variety-show): “If the wife had wished to
go to Sydney with the theatrical troupe, and her husband
had objected on the ground of the temptation to which
she would have been subjected, she might well have com-
plained that he was insulting her.” It is as well for the
reputation of Poverty Pointers generally that all the
Supreme Court Judges are not Maddens.
H.: “Re theatre accidents. I was in Melb. Royal when
Bandmann’s lago stabbed Mrs. Steele’s Emilia. She
uttered a painful 1 Ah ! ’ and the people applauded ; but
lago came to the footlights, imploring someone to fetch a
surgeon. I was also in Melb. Hayinarket when Walter
Hill (Harry’s father) met with his frightful accident, being
impaled by a spike while getting over a mock-wall. The
afterpiece, ‘ Ernani ’ burlesque, was not acted, but the
Argus reported that it came off indifferently, and made
no reference to the accident.”

cast ? Even Rignold is.

The Austral Salonists are now lionising Emelie Melville
and Shakespeare. The lady’s views on the Bard of A.
formed the staple of a recent afternoon talkee-talkee.
But why Shakespeare ? Isn’t it time some other Immortal
had a show ? William would come out so nice and reju-
venated if they gave him a rest for, say, a century or so.
Another fitting victim of a conspiracy of silence would be
Molifere.

The two ’Murkan dramatic companies now visiting this
hemisphere don’t go in for much mutual admiration. But
some of the members of one crowd agree with nearly all
of the other contingent in denouncing The Bulletin as a
scurrilous rag, for having said of them what they say of one
another.
Dear Bulletin,— A Poverty Point par. (Aug. 15) makes
it appear that Miss Yvonne Leverrier made her Melb.
dfibut at a Marshall Hall concert, and was afterwards cap-
tured for a Met. Liedertafel performance. This is incor-
rect. The Liedertafel introduced the young Sydney
pianiste to notice, with the result that the committee has
been violently reproached by a section of the Melb. press
for having ventured to look beyond the Vic. borders for
new talent. Sundry local teachers have pupils whom
they would like to put forward for advt. purposes.—E.
Nat Goodwin does not even acknowledge the authorship
of “The Ballad Monger” in his play-bills and programmes.
Several versions of the Gringoire story have been written.
If one mistakes not, the version Goodwin plays is that by
Walter Besant and W. H. Pollock, produced by Beerbohm
Tree in London five or six years ago. But why not say so
on the playbills?
Dr. Neild, the Melbourne surgeon and Shakespearian,
has an ambition to superintend the production of an open-
air representation of “As You Like It.” The difficulty is
to find a competent actress with the necessary beauty of
limb for Rosalind. The old doctor says he knows plenty
of good legs on clever girls, but the owners thereof will
only show them when necessary to their medical
advisers, and not to an admiring public. A bibliophile,
however, should know how to prize the sight of treasures
not revealed to the vulgar gaze; at least, that is the com-
fort his friends give him.
George Musgrove was so cocksure of Goodwin’s success
in Australia that he told the Mellikan man he would not
need to take more than two plays with him—“ Gilded
Fool ” and “In Mizzoura.” And the third week of the
season in Melbourne opened with “The Rivals.’

Dear Bulletin,— The assertion that George Rignold
was too old and too large for his part in “Cheer, Boys,
Cheer/ is unfair. It wasn’t a young romantic lover
character, but that of a grave, middle-aged, heavy swell.
In two or three scenes, particularly in the first act, the
actor was the right man in his proper place. By the way,
physical and mental worry have reduced G.R.’s solid flesh
of late, and his stage-figure is- ten years younger than it
looked a month ago.—B.A.
Goodwin has commissioned George Gordon to paint five
scenes for him to take back to America, in addition to the
same artist’s setting for “ The Gilded Fool,” which he has
purchased from the firm.
“ A nice little sugar-plum ” was J. C. Williamson’s
opinion of “ Romeo’s First Love.”
Miss Lillie Large, who d6butted at the Queen’s Hall,
London, is a Maoriland soprano, born at Napier, and
tutored by Framezzani. Her voice is light and flexible,
well under control, and used with intelligent feeling.
Predictions of celebrity for every ardent songstress who
takes the floor at a Melb. amateur concert are sent along
to .Tiie Bulletin in paragraphs starting with : “Yet an-
other budding Melba or Crossley ! ” This is rather vague
and unsatisfactory, especially in the case of “ buds ” quite
old enough to know which sort of famous vocalist they
mean to blossom into.
One of the “ illusions ” that Carl Hertz has brought
over with him is the kinetoscope, worked on the magic-
lantern principle, with figures developed to about two-
thirds of life-size. Judging from a private trial given at
Melb. Opera House the other night, these kinetoscopical
views on a sheet are not as clear, or natural in movement,
as the peep : show pictures produced by Edison’s original
apparatus, but the effect is wonderful—perhaps too won-
derful for nine out of ten persons in a theatre audience.
The trouble will be to make them realise that hundreds
of lightning photographs are employed in the representa-
tion of an omnibus travelling across London Bridge, or of
the sad sea waves rolling in upon a strip of sandy beach.
A forgotten place of amusement is the place between
the back of Melb. Theatre Royal and the hospital, with
Little Bourke-street at one end and Lonsdale at the other.
Now a livery stable, supplying horses for Bland Holt, it
was built as Rowe’s American Hippodrome, when Rowe
first returned from America, and afterwards it was the
Prince of Wales Theatre, tenanted by the Bros. Dick and
Fred Younge. Rowe came for the third time as an em-
ploy 6of Chiarini, arid was a broken-spirited man. Once
he made heaps of money in Melb., giving grandly to the
charities.
Wallace Brownlow has taken up a lease about eight
miles out of Kalgoorlie, and is working it himself in
between his fits of working at the old musical lode.
Westralian papers report a triumphant appearance of the
Brownlow Concert Co. at Kanowna, where they gave a
programme to suit the mining soul. The co. consisted
mainly of Wallace 8., Prof. Benjamin, the club manager,
and young Horace Destree, an ex-Melbourne amateur,
who played the accompaniments and sang about half-a-
dozen comic songs. The local critic, after expressing
regret that a gentleman with Mr. Destree’s voice should
waste it upon such inferior compositions as “ The
Hebrews’ Fancy Ball,” added, sorrowfully, “ he was en-
cored, however.”
Perhaps the most gory stage tragedy on record is one
that occurred in Sweden a few centuries ago. A Passion
play of “The Crucifixion” was being performed. A
favorite actor playing the part of a Roman soldier had to
feign to pierce the side of the actor on the cross. He was
so carried away by his part that he drove his spear in
reality between the ribs of the actor and killed him.
Whereupon King John 11., who was a spectator, in his
anger killed the actor who had made the blunder. The
people, vexed at the loss of a popular performer, set on
King John and cut his head off.
Melbourne of late seems to have been saving up. its
money for “ A Trip to Chinatown.”

Madame de Kontski, wife of the veteran pianist, is a
keen woman of business. A friend visited them the
morning after the Chevalier had played at the United
Arts Club, Sydney. “ What do you mean by keeping an
oldinan up all night?” she demanded. “My dear, my
dear,” said the Chevalier, “it gave them pleasure.”
“ Pleasure ! you ought to know better; a man of your
reputation' should never play anywhere unless he be .
paid.” The Chevalier is now ill at Adelaide. He con-.
tracted influenza, which has developed into pneumonia.

him.

Herr Benno Scherek, pianist to Trebelli’s Melbourne
concerts, first came into prominence before the Australian
public as Santley’s favored accompanist. His accompani-
ment of Gounod’s “ Nazareth ” was a perfect revelation to
amateurs. Later, Madame Urso, the violiniste, secured
his services on her Australian tour; and finally Scherek
settled down in Melbourne, which city he thinks affords
great opportunities for musicians from abroad.

cello.

gaol for a criminal assault at Mackay, has been
released after serving less than half his time.
%He will at once join the North Queensland
native police.” It sounds quite like old imes.
The Queensland native police has never been any-
thing much better than an organised gang of cut-
throats.

When Judges Windeyer and Innes took
leave of absence and Darley and Stephen resigned
from N.S.W. Law Consolidation Commission,
Premier Reid officially announced that the country
was done with the business, and added that it was
arrant folly to set about consolidating laws, most
of which he had determined to repeal or amend.
A few nights after Attorney-General Want said:
“ The Commission’s work is not over. It must be
taken up where it has been dropped and vigor-
ously carried on, but at considerably less expense.”
Then Quidnuncs asked : “ Which head of the
Ministry will prevail?” The appointment of C.
G. Heydon and recommencement of work proves
that in this case “Jack ” is something better than
his “master.”
The N.S.W. Ministerial song in ordering
that the building of the new Sydney Houses of
Parliament be at once proceeded with is that the
edifice may be useful for the Federal Parliament.
Which is calculated to promote Federation with a
vengeance!
As the Houses of Parliament are a national and
permanent concern involving a huge expenditure,
it will be simply monstrous to shut out, as pro-
posed, all designs save those of the Government
architect. Mr. Vernon is a man high up in the
estimation of his fellows, but he may not, after
all, be an absolute Michael Angelo ; also, he was
possibly appointed just as much because he had
plenty of social influence as because he was an
excellent and reliable architect. Lord and Lady
Carington are said to have smiled very sweetly on
the proposal to give Mr. Vernon his present post.

An English religious paper publishes an
extract from the letternf a Chartered Co.’s soldier
in South Africa :
It is grand fun potting niggers off and seeing them fall
like ninepins. . . . There have been two or three
caught in the town lately and shot as spies. There is
great excitement to see them shot. Crowds go out to see
it. They are stood ten yards off the firing party, who all
—eight or nine of them—blaze at them at once. It is
quite a nice sight out here now. One gets callous
and hardened, and does not object to seeing it. At home
now, I could not see a man hung for the world. Out here
it is an amusement. Yesterday three natives were hung
for being spies. They were marched out of town about
half-a-mile. Ropes were tied to branches of a tree, they
were made to climb up, the rope put round their necks
and made to jump off the tree and drop. One would not
for some time. He would talk to Mr. Colenbrander, who
asked him at last, “ Can you see Buluwayo ? ” The nigger
said “ Yes.” So Mr. Colenbrander said, “ Have a good
look at it, as you will never see it again.” So he did, then
said “ Good-bye, Mr. Colenbrander,” then jumped off.
The cheek of the sweep amused me !
“The cheek of the sweep amused me.” J. Bull
expects the nigger shall even be hanged respect-
fully.

The Broken Hill surgeon who boned a
cadaver, as a champion of the rights of all hos-
pital doctors, because the local coroner had
ordered an outside medico to perform the post-
mortem examination of a man who died in the
hospital, hit upon a brand-new artifice. This
question of professional etiquette has been dis-
turbed before, but the Broken Hill vertebra-
annexer is the original Columbus who made that
egg to stand on end.
There is good authority for saying that the
reason why the Imperial Govt, handed over Nor-
folk Island to N.S.W., which had not asked for
it, and declined to hand it over to Maoriland,
which had asked for it and was 400 miles nearer
to it than is N.S.W., is that Bull thinks Maori-
land is drunk with Socialism. N.S.W. is believed
to be more firmly rooted in the orthodoxy of Govern-
ment.
A new (protected) industry is about to start
in Sydney in the shape of a Lager Bier brewery.
Hitherto Victorian lager has had a large sale in
New South Wales, and has proved itself a light,
refreshing beverage eminently suited to the cli-
mate. Now-a local brewery co. is in course of
formation, and, the duty-excise on local beer being
considerably lower than the duty on the imported
article, the enterprise is expected to flourish.*
Baumann’s Cafd, Pitt-st., Sydney, with
its attractive luncheon and dining rooms for ladies
and gentlemen, has lately instituted a special din-
ner served every evening, at an inclusive tariff of
two shillings, for the convenience of theatre-
parties, &c. Ladies’ afternoon tea and dainty
cakes are a speciality evoking commendation.
Wedding-cakes and pastry of best quality are for-
warded, securely packed, to any part of the colony,
and catering of every description is undertaken
anywhere. *
Vic. Assembly has just discovered that it
pays civil servants <£loo,ooo a year in “ allow-
ances,’’which it wasn’t aware of, and a violent
attack of retrenchment is imminent. The item
which roused the bile of the House worst of all is
one concerning a certain railway engineer. In
the Patterson days the Assembly retrenched this
person from .£750 to ,£668. Then the Govt, gave
him a surreptitious allowance of £l5O a year “in
lieu of increase of salary,” so his screw was really
raised to £BlB, and the Assembly tears out great
handfuls of its hair and wants to know what is
the good of it professing to have control of the
public purse if things like this are to happen. It
took .£lOB off another man, and now it discovers
that he has <£l4o of allowances, and it finds that
several other men get <£4o a year for “ increased
cost of living,” whatever that may be.
Mr. Chas. Kerry, of the popular Sydney
photographic firm of Kerry & Co., has published a
series of most interesting photographs taken in the
little-known Kiandra district of N.S.W. Kiandra
is only 24 hours from Sydney, yet the winter of
the Australian Alps invests it with a mantle of
frozen cold seven months in the year. The 60 or
70 inhabitants, and half-dozen dogs and goats,
enjoy this ; and when the glass is 42° below zero
men, women, and children travel round on their
snow-shoes like greased lightning. This Arctic
region should become a veritable sanatorium for
the heat-stricken of other districts; there for
dinner if you eat two fowls you feel light and
happy afterwards ; you melt your frozen nose and
ears and cheque before hotel furnaces as big as the
Prophet Daniel’s ; you snow-shoe with the grac<-
of youth and beauty if you are built that way,
and if not you squat toad-fashion on your
snow - shoes and toboggan down the slippery
dopes, slump. And sleep there is sleep’s ideal.
There have been some heavy falls of snow at
Kiandra, particularly in ’94. That winter old
Yorky, a local identity, was snow-shoeing home
one moonlight night. Guide-postsand telegraph
posts were buried ; Yorky was a mere wandering
speck upon a vast expanse of white. The sense of
dazzling light filled his soul with gloom ; no trees
being visible he was bushed. But just at the
moment of despair he caught sight of a trail of
smoke issuing from the snow. He steered over
to it, slowed up, stopped. Glory ! There beneath
him was a luxurious steak grilling over the hotel
kitchen-fire. He slid down the chimney to supper.
The Eucumbene rivulet runs right through
Kiandra ;itis a tributary of the Snowy. Most
of the Kiandra men are miners. When Sydney
was baking on 108° day, Kiandra was calling its
84° “ d -d ’ot. ’ ’

Australian University Library after being “out ”
34 years 7 months and 23 days. The hero of this
loan is one of the professors, who has delivered
precisely the same set of lectures for 40 years and
jver.

N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench, drawing
salaries to just on £19,000 a-year, shaped for
work in this fashion one day last week: Owen,
Stephen and Simpson fit to sit up ; Darley and
Manning down with grip or something; and
Windeyer and Innes abroad, one convalescing and
one hunting for a billet with more glory in it.
Emergency-men Cohen and Rogers were all there,
®f course; but they are not counted in the sum
the country regularly pays for Supreme Court
Bench-work.
One day in N.S.W. Supreme Court Salo-
mons, Q.C., was on one side of a case and Cohen
on the other, and Salomons quoted something
from a Bacon’s report, which he handed straight
to the Bench, instead of letting it go through
Cohen. “I do not like to offer Bacon to rny
learned friend,” said Salomons; “Mr. Cohen is
most orthodox.”
Our old, but unvenerable, fat friend,
Arthur Orton, offers to retract his precious “ con-
fessions ” of a year ago and produce new evidence
of his rights to the Tichborne baronetcy—if he
can get a Juggins to finance him. The confessions
were lies, no doubt, but the truth concerning the
Claimant would not be of interest to anybody
nowadays. Besides, he has forgotten most of the
truth.
Geoffrey Drage,
recently elected
English M.P., and
formerly secretary
of the big Labor
Commission, was, it
is more than sus-
pected, a visitor to
Australia four or
five years ago. At
all events, a young
man of the most
pronounced haw-
ha wish Oxonian
type called in a
mysterious fashion
upon several promh
nent and would-be
prominent people
in two or three dif-
ferent provinces,
and on a great
many people who
were officially in-
terested in educa-
tion, “lower-class”
politics, and in so-
ciological work, and
to most of the peo-
ple he honored with
a call he gave a
book. This was the
novel, “Cyril,’’the
late editions of
which appear with
Drage’s name on
the title-page. “Cy-
ril ” is a pnggishly-
clever but terribly
diffuse study of so-
ciety and politics,
British and Conti-
nental, and was re-
ceived with a good
deal of adulation as
priggish as many of
the chapters of the
book. The interest
of Mr. Drage’s visit
for Australians lay in the belief that he was
supposed to have very high official sanction—of
the sort that can be repudiated if necessary—for
his attempt to discover the actual state of opinion
here with reference to a Royal personage as
Governor-General of the projected Federation. So
much mystery surrounded the visitor’s movements,
and so much uncertainty prevailed as to his name
that, if it were not that the book bore the in-
scription “from the author,” doubt would still
exist as to who the man was. It was suggested,
indeed, that he himself was a “ Royalty ” incog.,
but his obvious braininesß discounted that sup-
position.

Melbourne book-shops are now flowing
over with Stead’s twopenny condensed extracts of
the works of eminent authors. The cold cheek of
the man ! He would, if required, condense the
Lord’s Prayer or the Ten Commandments without
turning a hair. And so, it is seriously suspected,
would his rev. Melbourne Editor Fitchett.
A newly-arrived tall-hatted candidate for
literary employment in a leading Sydney news-
paper-office was lately asked, ‘ ‘ Are you an M. A. ? ”
Next, “ Are you a B. A. ? ” Next, “ Are you an
LL.D., or anything of that sort?” And, finally,
“ Are you a reverend of any kind ? ” The
answers were all “ No’s,” and, soon after, a sad-
faced man passed down the stairs and out into the
cold street.
Henry Lucy in S.M. Herald :
Lady Pender reached the apex of her career one night
when at her own house, at a party given to meet Stanley
just home from having discovered Emin Pasha, the King
of the Belgians conducted her to the upper room.
A nice thing to print about an unoffending lady
in a country perfectly acquainted with the reputa-
tion of the King of the Belgians.
Chief-Justice Berkeley, of Fiji, sat with
N.S.W. judges in Banco one day last week. Talk-
ing of his predecessor, St. Julian, the first Chief
Justice of Fiji, Mr. Berkeley said that the work
of the office is much less distracting and onerous
now than in St. Julian’s day. He professed to be
greatly entertained by the gossip which still
attaches to the name of the ex-Sydney law-reporter
whom King Thakombau installed as chief admin-
istrator of his country’s laws.

Melb. demise : County Court Judge Bin-
don’s widow. Bindon was a worthy, poll-parroty,
cockatoo-faced fogey, who was satirised in the
pantomimes for his shocking bad hats. Though
very correct in demeanour, he scandalised Gipps-
land, on circuit, by shooting little birds on Sun-
day. His son, barrister Bindon, instructed by
Gaunson, defended Ned Kelly, not quite in the
Aspinall way, wherefore, partly, Gaunson pleaded
that Ned ought to escape the rope.
Somebody has remarked, re the appro-
priateness of names, that one naturally associates
the name Wragg with illegitimacy and sordid
suicide or child-murder. More significant far are
the names of two children just committed to the
care of Vic. neglected children’s department.
They were respectively 7 and 8 years old, and had
been living in a house with no furniture, and only
half a quilt and two bran-bags to cover them.
Their grandfather, aged 80, could sometimes earn
1/6 per week, sometimes nothing. Their mother
appears to have deserted them. Their names are
George Grieve and Elsie May Grieve.

Premier Reid never eats baker’s bread,
in consequence of a disgusting sight he saw in a
bakehouse, where a drunken baker was work-
ing, some years ago. G.H.R. ate no bread next
day, and when on tour generally contrives to take
with him a small supply of home-made. Owing to
a somewhat cognate occurrence, not unconnected
with a dairy presided over by two sore-eyed black
gins, Mr. Reid’s immediate predecessor in office,
Parkes, for some years took no milk in his tea.

When Dick Seddon, M.L.’s present Pre-
mier, came over from Melbourne to the West
Coast in ’66 and swung a lusty pick-and-shovel on
the Greenstone rush, he chummed in casually with
two brothers on the next claim. One of them waa
named Dan ; the other, as a matter of course, was
Bill. After a while, Bill resolved to take a run
“ home,” from which he had been many yeaija
absent, and there he learned that his sister had
married one Seddon, and that a large, hulking soji
had grown up and recently departed for Maorj-
land. Seddon had accidentally run up against his
own uncle Bill and his other without any
one of the party understanding the situation.

tors of the various God-fearing N.S.W. Ministers
as to the medium in which their remarkable heads
should be finally “ done ” with a view to preserva-
tion in imperishable form. As far as Reid is con-
cerned, it’s a toss-up between soap and brass.
Jpe Cook might be done in sealing-wax—post-office
sealing-wax; Gould in alabaster, as the nearest
approach to ice; Garrard ip gold without alloy,
as a specially God-fearing Minister ; Brunker in
tallow; Carruthers in putty; Syd. Smith, ajs
Minister for Agriculture,- might have his head
carved out ;of a prize pumpkin; anjd Jack Wanjfc
should be done in common clay, as'the only self-
oonfessed human being in the lot.
The Samuel Marsden memorial business is
not likely to “catch on ’’ to any dangerous extent.
Firstly, many classed as his admirers know that
his character won’t bear close .'examination.
Secondly, there are for all, except greedy persons,
quite enough memorials of the rev. -ex-blacksmith
in Australia and elsewhere', In the church of his
native village in England there is a tablet to him ;
St. John’s, Parramatta, has a memorial; and All
Saints church at Marsfield was built to honor him.
Besides, Dean Cowper, of St. Andrew’s, Sydney,
is a living memorial of the rough-and-ready and
often ferocious politipo-bvangelifet, for it was
Marsden who took away the dean’s father from
rural England and started the family in the shep-
herd business on the west of Wyn’yard-square.
About £BOOO has been recovered out of
the ,£lO,OOO in gold which went down in the
Catterthun many months ago. Considering the
depth—just on 160 ft. —at which the divers had to
work, the strong currents, and the fact that the
wreck lay in the open waters of the Tasman Sea,
it was the most remarkable diving feat on record,
and the two men, Briggs and May, who fetched
up the lost bullion richly deserve tne <£7so apiece
which they receive. It is eerie work, wandering
among the recesses of a lost ship on the sea bottom
—especially a ship whose passengers mostly went
down with her, so that their sodden bones are liable
to be met with at any turn in the regions below
decks—where the big shark and the conger-eel and
octopus come to eat the sunken crew. Diving with
modern appliances is a solemn way of making a
living, and the man who goes dynamiting Chubb
locks 50 yards below the surface and poking round
in the green twilight at the bottom of the ocean
looking for lost treasure must, if he possesses the
imagination of a normal human being, deserve all
the money he makes at his industry. The expense
of the prolonged search for the Catterthun’s gold
was great, but still there is a large profit left
in the enterprise. The underwriters are now sell-
ing a lot of the recovered sovereigns at a guinea
each, as mementoes —the odd shilling going in
aid of the Shipwreck Relief Society.

POLITICAL
POINTS
“ Freetrade can never be other than a
variable expedient.”—Frederick Greenwood.
The difference between the Devil and the
politician is that the politician is blacker than he
is painted.
Minister Young says the Reid Govt, will
last till 1900. That would mean altogether a con-
secutive six years’rule and majorities in at least
three consecutive general elections.
Brisbane Courier has the front to propose
a second vote for Ministers and ex-Ministers of
the. Crown, and talks grandiloquently about the
“ high worth of their patriotism and patent record
of service done.”
All the good-looking men in S.A. Assembly
are against woman-suffrage ; and the hard-faced
politicians who would ornament a chain-gang are
for it almost to a man. The ugliest politicians
are.the most enthusiastic on the subject.
Old Frederick Sargood, M.L.C., alleges
that Vic.. Legislative Council “ rests on a sound
democratic basis.” Think of the dreadful Sargood
—the man who is against every semblance of re-
form—being on a democratic basis ! He would be
more comfortable on an iron spike.
N.S.W. Premier Reid went to the country
last year on the burning question of reforming the
Leg. Council. He never attempted to reform it.
He merely deformed it. To-day the Council is as
cantankerous and as people-defying as ever. Reid
is keeping “ reform of the Council ” as an ever-
lasting trump-card up his sleeve.
The electors of Melbourne province show
no disposition to shift Robt. Reid from the Leg.
Council. Flinders Lane runs the show with the
assistance of cherubic Dr. Bevan, and the soft-
goods representative will be returned unopposed,
seemingly. Robt. Reid, as a “ successful public
man,” is an irritating mystery to a good many
people.
“HIS MATE’S ALIVE!” — From, Henry Lawson’s volume of
sketches , “ While the Billy Boils.”
The stock inspectors appointed by Q.
Govt, to cope with the tick trouble appear to be
largely composed of needy Ministerial hangers-on,
with no knowledge of or fitness for the work.
Victoria lias been
crying out for years
past for a reform of
the insolvency law
and the law relating
to companies, yet the
measures dealing
with these two re
forms were on the
point of being strand-
ed 2C times over for
want of a quorum.
Both bills struggled
through comm i ttee
in an almost empty
House, and all t! >e
work on them was
done by about a
dozen members. The
rest stayed away—■
except at divisions
because they didn’t
care for such dreary
work, or didn t un-
derstand it, and were
too lazy or too stupid
to learn. But if the
Council throws out
these two bills or
amends them out of
all shape, reckoning
that where the As-
sembly is so indif-
ferent it can do
pretty much as it
pleases, the howl of
the men who stayed
away will rend the
heavens, and they
will want to know
why the Government
they are too tired to
support doesn’t rise
and do something
great and awful at
once.
Brisbane Courier pleading for a plurality
of votes :
The idle, self-indulgent, discontented elements in the
community die out or migrate. The industrious, thrifty,
thoughtful element stays on. Therefore, we say let a
citizen of twenty years’ standing have a second vote.
In every Bananaland coast-town there are hun-
dreds of industrious, thrifty citizens who are con-
tinually transporting themselves, and often their
families, in the search for work; there are also
hundreds of loafers and drunkards who live for
twenty years on the same old booze, in the same
old street, within hail of the same old pub. . . .
Evidently Queensland is booked shortly for a
Redistribution of Seats Bill and a fancy franchise.
Considering
that the N.S.W.
gaols yield practi-
cally nothing in the
shape of cash value,
and that the prison-
ers are not employed
on any kind of work
that gives a decent
return for - their
labor, and that
whenever any at-
tempt is made to
employ them in pay-
ing occupations a
wail goes up about
them competing
with free labor out-
si d e considering
all these things, The
Bulletin suggests
salt-making as an
industry which
might fill a large
gap. N.S.W. spends
about £60,000 a year
on imported salt. If
importation was
wholly prohibited,
and salt made a
State monopoly, and
the industry run
wholly by prison
labor, it would com-
pete with no free in-
dustry in- the pro-
vince. And if the
Prison department,
with no wages to
pay, and no eight-
hour movement, and
no strikes, couldn’t
supply the country
at present prices or
even less, then some-
body evidently
wants to be kicked
hard.
English Co-
operative Delegate
Clay : “We think
it is only right that
we should do some-
thing with our own
colonies in prefer-
ence to sending so
much money to other
countries.” “ Our
own colonies! ” The
voice of the world-
owning Hinglishman ! Australia owns nothing—
not even herself.
A rude and effective assault was lately made
bySleath, M.L.A.,upon the pious self-complacency
of N.S.W. Col. Sec. Brunker during the debate
on the financial statement. Sleath described Mr.
Brunker as an incompetent humbug who lived on
his hair and his eternal professions of integrity.
As it was delivered in Committee early in the
morning it was not reported, but it was un-
doubtedly the most trenchant attack yet made on
the ill-founded political reputation of the Colonial
Secretary.

—wet seasons—suckers and seedlings—pine scrub
—Cumberland—fluke—worms—staggers yellows
