THE BIRD-CAGE,—PRETTY POLLY!
Remarkable how public opinion influences
even the Law. Melb. Trades Hall unionists now
freely stick about posters, warning the public
against certain employers, by.name.
A custom is springing up in certain N.S.W.
Protectionist quarters for leading men who are
announced to speak at meetings to come in late
and attract applause on squeezing on to the plat-
form. One ex-M.P. for Northumberland in recent
years carried this to a fine art, and the expression
of startled surprise which overspread his counten-
ance on finding himself an object of applause
would have done credit even to George Rignold.
Leg. Council Franchise Extension Bill,
which would have enfranchised 15,000 lease-
holders and others, was carried in S. A. Council by
12 votes to 11, but as an absolute majority of the
House was needed, the measure lapses for this
session. The Kingston Govt, has been much
blamed for not originating the Bill in the Assem-
bly, as there is a large majority in favor of it in
that chamber. Magarey and Campbell, M’sL.C.,
alleged democrats, voted with the Ass party.
Vic. Government is going to try its hand
at another Bill for abolishing the property suf-
frage and giving one vote to every man and woman
in the province, and the Council is getting ready
to heave out the hated measure once more. The
struggle between the two Houses in Victoria is
the fight between the elephant and the whale over
again—a combat between the man who has no
hands and the one who has no feet. The One-
adult-one-vote Bill should bring things to a crisis
if the two institutions could get at each other—
but, unluckily, they oan’t.
An old-established Kyneton (Vic.) solicitor
was dragged up the other day before Judge
Williams for retaining a lady-client’s money ever
since 1890, and flatly refusing to give any account
of it. He was suspended for 12 months certain,
and thereafter further suspended until he paid up
the ill-gotten coin. Under these arrangements he
has no interest in paying the money until his 12
months’ suspension is just expiring, and the client
may look for her cash in August, 1897. What
other profession is there in which a man who has
misappropriated trust money for six years or
so, gets another 12 months in which to settle up ?
It is understood that Vic. Assembly will
dissolve next April if things go well, and if “things
go badly it may dissolve earlier-. The general im-
pression is that the batch of Leg. Councillors who
go for re-election in Sept., and who include many
of the Government’s most inveterate opponents,
will mostly be returned almost unopposed. And
the further impression is that the same people
who return them will send Turner back in April
with a big majority, and having thus fixed up the
same old deadlock once more will want to know
why the Government doesn’t “do something.”
The Vic. public apparently can’t understand that
so long as it lets the same fossils drift back to the
Council no Government can do much. The one
effective remedy would be to provide on the esti-
mates a salary of £4OO or .£5OO a year for each
Councillor. Then a rush for the M.L.C.-ships
would set in that would sweep Fatman right off
his legs. It would take about £5OO a year to do
it; the constituencies are so much larger and elec-
tions consequently so much more expensive than
those for the Assembly that a big salary is neces-
sary.
B. R. Wise writes to Sydney daily press
rubbing salt into the wounds inflicted upon Reid
by M‘Millan, and thus brings into view a political
combination, on which Reid will have to keep his
weather-eye. Wise and M‘Millan have never
been very close j>olitical associates, representing
as they do two diverse streams of political thought
on Freetrade. M‘Millan stands for “sound and
safe ” conservative legislation, with no disinclina-
tion to revenue-tariffism, tempered by M‘Arthur
and Co., and “good Government”; while Wise
stands for speculative Henry Georgeism, and
advanced taxation with “ exemptions on incomes
of ability.” For the rest, both men are far abler
Rarliamentarians than any on Reid’s side of the
House, and outside command two different kinds
of support, which may or may not be reconcil-
able. They both have a legacy of revenge from
their old chief, Parkes ; to say nothing of a big
personal score to be wiped out. Both were offered
the leadership of the Freetrade party when Parkes
retired in a huff and it was going begging. Both
refused, on the evident assumption that Parkes
was indispensable. All these little rivers run into
one big stream, and Reid will have to fight for
dear life when waters are not so smooth as now.
While M‘Millan and Reid are making and
losing reputations—the public can take its choice
which loses and which gains—over the financial
position of N.S.W., the Opposition financiers pre-
serve a judicious silence on details. Indeed, Lyne
and See were at sixes and sevens on the actual
delinquencies of the Treasurer in their speeches,
and both disagreed with M'Millan, while all of
them were at loggerheads with Reid. What a
puzzle N.S.W. finance must be when three past or
present Treasurers and an ex-Minister for Works
are making the air blue with their contradictions !
When Chinese who cross over into N.Q.
are captured and found to have no money, what
is done with them? “They are detained in cus-
tody,” is the usual Governmental reply. But
somebody asked Home Secretary Tozer the other
day—“ How long?” And then the beautiful
State system intended to put practical difficulties
in the way of the Chinesing of Australia fell to
pieces. “ What’s the use of keeping them in cus-
tody when they have neither food nor money?”
answered Tozer. “Of course, they pass out. into
the general population to make a living for them-
selves.”
There is a puncture in the tyre of the Vic.
Parliamentary Labor-party. The feeling between
I lancock and Prendergast was illustrated a few
nights since. Prendergast was raising a stonewall,
but Hancock wanted to catch his last train home.
“ Cut it short,” said Hancock. “ The hon. mem-
ber who interrupts is very rarely here at this time
of night,” said Prendergast. “It would be a
good thing if you weren’t,” retorted Hancock.
Now the two champions of Labor mutually de-
nounce each other to their respective groups of
adherents. Yet the quarrel is premature. Tren-
with’s shoes are not empty yet, and if they were,
neither Hancock nor Prendergast is fit to fill them.
All the reasons which determined the U.S.
Constitution-makers to deny to Washington, the
seat of Federal Government, a vote in the national
elections apply with equal force to the city in
which the Parliament of a Federated Australia
shall assemble. Have those Sydney Exhibition-
artificers who endeavor to justify the erection of
their doll’s-house by hinting that Sydney may be-
come the seat of Australian Government given the
necessary amount of thought to this aspect of the
business ? Are Sydney and its suburbs likely to
accept disfranchisement even for the sake of hous-
ing the national representatives? This question
should be definitely settled before one pound of
money is voted for the construction of a building,
one of whose uses, the proprietors say, will be to
serve as a Federal Parliament House.

“The Legislative Council of Victoria is now

SARGOOD, M.L.C.
Ex-Minister Yale, who has just quitted
the Turner (Vic.) Cabinet, says that he pointed
out how at least ,£lOO,OOO a year more could be
saved on the civil service, but his colleagues lacked
the grit to carry out his plans of retrenchment.
Also, that he showed a possible saving of .£50,000
a year in the Post-office alone, whereupon that
frivolous Mike, Postmaster-Gen. Duffy, sacked
one man. He wasn’t one of the men recom-
mended for retrenchment, and he was the post-
master in Yale’s own electorate. Whereupon Vale
threw his portfolio at Duffy’s head and left.

of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of G6oft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 0, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; !»,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11 a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books, Ihe
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

Alexander craigmile, a 6ft. irish-
man, arrived in Melbourne about 1858, being
then about 18 years of age. His younger brother,
CHARLES CRAIGMILE, came to the colonies
later. Wanted news of either by their aged
mother, Will any kind friend please send in-
formation to “ 0,” Box 469, Sydney.

A Mrs. Charles Comstock, of Fisher s
Corners, Michigan, U.S.A., has given birth to
seven children at one confinement —four girls and
three boys. All doing well. The record.
A German, charged before Bundaberg (Q.)
Bench with the larceny as a bailee of <£so, en-
trusted to him by a kanaka for safe-keeping, cross-
examined Tommy Tanna : “ Didn’t you give me
the money to inwest for you in my business ? ”
Tommy: “Yah.” Prosecuting counsel: “Do
you understand what that fellow mean when he
say inwest?” Tommy: “My word ; that b-
fellow been waste my money ever since I give it to
him.” Prisoner committed, but duly acquitted by
a jury of his “ fellow-countrymen.”

‘ ‘ Ceremonials are very well to look at, but
I can imagine nothing more uninteresting than to
read exhaustive and exhausting accounts of them.
A sincere, straightforward, historical narrative
would be unobjectionable, but it is part of the
conventionality of the occasion that the scribe
should write in a vein of exalted and insincere
adulation, leaving on the mind of the independent
reader a curious impression of pretentious servility
and snobbishness.” —A London journalist on the
Czar's coronation mummery.
Ed. Bulletin, —If anything could surprise
me,, your par. of Aug. 1 would do so. I am
charged with anarchial tendencies, and this after
many years of stubbornly fighting that madness.
The next thing will be to accuse me of being a
full-blown Roman Catholic. True, the row in our
association did spring out of practical anarchy,
certain members having developed that disease
badly, and vowing to drive me out of Melbourne
because I laughed at their fad and otherwise
opposed them. The hall was not half, but quite,
built ; and I lectured in it from May, ’B9, to
March, ’9l. When I tell you that our law-s'iit
against (he trustees was the very first case tried
by the now notorious Madden, you will fully
understand our failure. The other Judges had
not grown independent of the Chief when our
appeal against his decision was made; and they
reluctantly and with damning admissions con-
firmed his decision. Ino more expect to get jus-
tice from our Judges than to become a bishop.
The poetical embellishments re my laughing out of
the window and sitting weeping and raving amid
the ruins are not bad for romance, and they tend
to show that invention is not a lost art or “ gift ”
in literature. —Jos. Syhes.
The Illinois Staats Zeitung, a Chicago Tory
paper, thus advises: “Just as the corn-louse and
similar insects are driven out by setting against
them certain other insects that devour them and
their eggs, so should the State cultivate and rear
anarchists in the principal nests of Socialism, and
leave to the anarchists the work of destroying
socialists. The anarchists will do the work much
more thoroughly than either police or district
attorneys.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The argument re the inclusion of Ranjitsinjhi
in the English test-team, that the Indian prince learnt all
his cricket in England does not affect the matter,” re-
marks “ Australian Abroad ”to The Bulletin. “It is his
national physical characteristics, his wonderfully supple
wrist and keen eye, that make him such a clever batsman.
“Clem Hill was nearly being omitted from
the Australian team in the second test-match ; he has of
late so frequently failed through a desire to score by pul-
ling straight balls to leg.
“Jones, before the second test-match v. Eng-
land at Manchester, complained of having strained the
muscles of his side. A day’s rest and liniment did him
little good, but it was finally decided that he must be
played at all risks, otherwise, with Eady disabled (a
strained arm), the Australians would be absolutely without
fast bowling.
“ Trott stood out of the Leicester match and
went to see the Stanbury-Harding boat-race ; Giffen con-
sequently captained the team. He was wonderfully success-
ful bowling in the first innings, when he secured 8 for 30
runs; and in the second he got 6 for 89. He, however, kept
himself on from beginning to end of the second, and in
the last hour he was plainly tired. The last Leicester
man came in at 5.30 and stumps were to be drawn at 6.
If the last two men played out time it was a draw, if not
it was an Australian victory by an innings and 300 runs or
so. At one end Giffen gave Donnan a few overs, then
Eady an over or two, but he himself kept pegging away
at the other end. Trumble ought certainly to have had
more of a chance than two overs at such a time, and this
craze of Giffen’s for bowling himself again shows how im-
possible he is for a captain.
“Old Trafford wicket is always a trifle slow,
otherwise it could not possibly have been better than for
the second test-match. Trott won the toss, and had a
good advantage in getting first knock. The weather was
bright and charming as far as Manchester weather can be,
and there were fully 15,000 people present when Iredale
snicked Richardson for four the first ball of the match.
“ Against Hampshire the Australians made 462;
against the Players, 454; and against Leicester, 584; or
exactly 1500 runs in three consecutive innings—a record
performance.
“ The receipts of the Australian team up to the
close of the Hampshire match—half the tour—were £lOOO
ahead of the ’93 takings. If this increase is kept up, and
it probably will be, for the team is now much talked of,
some of the careful ones (and r.here are three or four) will
have a big lump to draw at the end of the trip.
‘ ‘ Fourteen Englishmen attended at Old Trafford
for the ‘ All-England ’ (assisted by Ranjitsinjhi) second
test-match. Grace made the final selection half-an-hour
before play began. He decided to leave out Lohmann,
Pougher, and Mold. Pougher we saw get a nasty blow on
the knee the day before at Leicester from Donnan’s bowl-
ing; but it was wonderful with what unanimity the three
omitted professionals “ told the old man my leg was bad,
or my arm was bad,” as the case may be. The profes-
sionals requested to attend to plav if called upon get
their money whether or no, but the advt. of the inclusion
in an English team—and the honor —is highly prized.
Double fees (£10) are paid for these matches.
“On no ground in England do the Gentlemen
and Professionals come out at the same gate ; nor do they
dine together. For instance, E. Jones, Esq., of Broken
Hill, would never be called upon to eat at the same table
as Richardson, the Surrey professional.
“At Lord’s, 43 reporters filled the press-box
during the first test-match, while 44 found places during
the second at Manchester. Of these, two men represent-
ing Pardon’s Cricket Reporting Agency (late Wisden’s)
and two men representing the Sportsman Agency supply
over 80 papers with results. In addition there are other
smaller agencies and perhaps 30 pressmen on the ground
who never enter the box.
“When Iredale had made his hundred in the
second test-match he walked the full length of the wicket
to Trott, who was in with him. They shook hands effu-
sively. Whether this was all Iredale went for or whether
he wanted to borrow something from his captain on the
strength of it, the great crowd of 25,000 spectators, who
had assembled by that time, could not tell.
“ And the boys rolled up . . the bloomin’ sacri-
fice to see ” on the Thames between Putney and Mort-
lake on 13th July. It was seriously stated and universally
accepted as correct that Stanbury’s ‘innards’ were so
flaked with fat that, at every st roke he pulled, the liquefied
margarine either clogged the valves of the heart or so
copiously exuded from his pores that he was unable to
get a body-purchase from which to ‘ put his shoulders
into it.’ Thus the only advantage the Australian pos-
sessed was as an oil-spray in case of ‘ troubled waters,’
and the ‘ Little Wonder’s ’ lightness was thought likely to
counterbalance that by enabling him to skim, like an
arrow, on the crests of the waves. Every paper that
ventured an opinion gave the muscular little Englishman
as the winner, and yet he was hopelessly outclassed, and
beaten after the first score of strokes.
* * * *
“ Stanbury, by so easily beating Harding, has
lost all chance of rowing any other Englishman.. Atone
point he rested on his sculls for a few moments and the
crowd shouted exultingly : ‘ He’s done ! he’s done ! ’ but
their ecstasy was very short, for Jim, putting in three
long, powerful strokes, immediately pulled up the two or
three lengths by which his lead had been reduced. There
was a lot of talk about Harding being able to beat Stan-
bury, but bookmakers of any repute would not lay against
the Australian. The match is not mentioned now, for
Englishmen really can’t stand a licking. Had Harding
won he would have been flooded with challenges.”
“Hammersmith Bridge, after the Stanbury-
Harding Chinaman cortfege : Disgusted pearl-buttoned,
over-clothed British workman removes his pipe, relieves
his mouth and his feelings in order—‘ Well, I’m con-
demned ! Pore old John Bull wopped agine ! Hit’s my
opinyun that in hathletiks John Bull hain’t no crimsun
clars ! ’
“At Henley, the four-oar races were rowed
without cox’uns, the steering being managed by rudder-
lines on the stroke’s feet. The Yale College (U.S.) crew
raced in a paper-boat, and might as well have been made
of paper for the sorry show they gave. When is Australia
to send a crew ?”

aggregate compiled during any Australian cricketing tour
in England.

George Lohmann has been quite a failure
against the Australians this time—bowling average poor

* * * *
Little Abel will come out with the best average
among the English pros, this season for all matches, as
well as the Anglo-Australian events. And his grim com-
rade, Richardson, stands out, after all, as the only bowler
who has always troubled the visitors on good wickets
and the most likely to be chosen for the English team
that will attack Australia next year.
* * * *
“ In sturdiness and evenness of form the Aus-
tralians have proved themselves superior to the best
eleven that could be chosen to represent All England ”
says London Daily Chronicle. Which nobody can deny
If Stoddart’s next team is to win test-matches on hard
Australian turf he will have to bring six or seven of the
very best English batsmen who can resist the temptation
to make uppish strokes. Looking back at the names of
Stoddy’s last crowd, it seems wonderful that they should
have won the rubber, a success due partly to the cap-
tain’s example in playing a much less dashing game than
he is noted for at Home, and partly to the comparative
softness of Australian bowling at that time. Brown, it
seems, takes no credit for winning the final test in Mel-
bourne. He reckons that the bowling on the last day was
ridiculously easy to hit, and he shone merety in the
matter of making up his mind to whack it about.
* * * *
Bails: “The compiler of the World’s Cricket
Eleven forgot a wicket-keep.”
* * * *
In a match between Cambridge University and
A. J. Webbe’s Eleven, young Albert Trott was the only
professional engaged. He had the distinction of being
the only player who didn’t get a “Mr.” before his name
in the newspaper reports.
* * * *
Geo. Giffen told the writer, the day before he
left for England, that, every man playing to form, the
present Australian team was (from a touring point of
view) the strongest batting combination in the annals of
cricket.
» * * *
The sun shone on last Saturday’s meeting of
N.S. W. Wheelmen’s League, and the muscle-worshippers
got filled to the brim with the excitement of sensational
finishes. Had gods been racing instead of men, the
leather-lunged 15,000 could not more frantically have
hailed each victor.
There was a tremendous outburst when Mead-
ham (N.S. W.) and Walne (Q.) fought desperately down the
straight for victory in the August Handicap final. Walne
won—not by a half-inch, but by three-eighths. As he
flew over the last 100yds. he could hear the mighty voice
of the Sydney barraekers bellowing “Meadham! Mead-
ham! Meadham wins!” “D d if he does!” said
Walne to himself, and he set his teeth and lasted out the
challenge. Wally Kerr, one of the coolest and best judges
of a race, was certain that Meadham won. But the
majority of those in a proper position to judge gave the
win to Walne. Meadham, who once more showed he had
no bundle, thought he had made a dead-heat of it.
Davison (N.S.W.) won the Winter Stakes very
easily, as anticipated ; the only thing he appears to re-
quire is a block of ice inside his cap. He bullocks instead
of racing with judgment; in his August Handicap heat
he made the pace for Meadham and O’Brien right through,
and once in the straight for home those clever riders
downed him for his pains.
The great Bilmai'tin was himself again last
Saturday, and had he not been badly blocked several
times in the final of the Aug. handicap he might have
been first instead of fourth. But in the Ten-mile Scratch
race he plugged along in his customary resolute style,
always hanging just “ thar,” or jigging suddenly to the
front and then as suddenly dawdling while in the lead —
a dodge to slow the race. And when the last lap came
he set-to with Martinic grimness, and neither M'Donnell
(Amor.) nor Body (M.L.) could challenge him in the
straight.
This is the first time that Martin has met
M'Donnell in a long distance. M'Donnell proved himself
a cool, clever rider, but he will have to lay in more sprint
and stamina to beat Martin in their match. The only
reason why Martin should not beat M'Donnell is because
the famous Bill has now been training three years with-
out one proper interval of holiday. Yet, judged by the
way Martin collared his field in the first two laps in his
heat of the August Handicap, he is not stale.
Martin is a rare good horse to carry anybody’s
money, because he has a happy knack of “getting
there.” When an expert boxer went for the uninitiated
William one day in the dressing-room, M. crowded
in and slogged off skin and hair most unscientifi-
cally. But he got there. The other man was carried
home for repairs, but W.M. went out on his cycle with
his iron jaw set for victory.
So far Martin is the brainiest and best-lived
cyclist in Australia. He does exactly the things he ought
to do, and he leaves undone the things he ought not to
do ; he goes to bed every night at 10 ; and he realises that
a racing cyclist’s biggest bundle is a girl.
Ken Lewis (M.L.) rode a very lucky and un-
lucky race in his heat of the August Handicap. He stole
up and up and up—on the inside all the way, and was
winning easily when Middleton (Vic.) rushed past to
snatch a win.
Walne (Q.) won his heat of the August Handi-
cap (1 mile), won that race’s final (£50); and by a magni-
ficent sprint won the Final Handicap, 3 miles (£l5). He is
a light, wiry, compact athlete—has a trunk as round as a
gum-tree.
Quong Tart, the starter, had to go near the
crowd once when firing the pistol. He remarked after-
wards : “ They chaff me over there ; ‘ Scotch Haggis,’
they say ; ‘ Cornin’ thro’ the Rye,’ Scotch gammon, and
so on ; I laugh. ‘ All li, ole f’lah,’ I say.”
The hearts of the quad could be heard thump-
ing against their ribs, and those of the spectators stood
still, as, when the four riders were pacing Don Walker
(Vic.) in the half-mile dash, James, the steersman, missing
his pedal caused the machine to swerve and rock fear-
fully. To have come down at that pacing gait would
well, it would have been a splash advt. for the ambulance
corps, which takes a benefit shortly. Walker did the
half-mile in lmin. 0 2-ssec. ; the rest of the half mile
dashes were struck out of the programme.
Middleton (Vic.), after he and Lewis had won
the Tandem Race, ground his teeth and shook his fist at
Elliott and M'Coonibe, who ran second. A lap and a-half
from home the latter pair nipped across Lewis and Mid. so
sharply that a smash was averted by a hair’s-breath. And
so, having won, Middleton bounced off his machine, and
with the fire of battle in his eye but friends swarmed
round.
* * * *
Sydney footballers are making wild efforts to
reach Melb. fight-record standard. On Saturday (Aug. 17)
a losing team (Carolinas) attacked the umpire, who was
saved by a policeman. The barraekers of the other club
were, however, knocked down, and a free fight with bricks
followed. The barraekers fled to an empty house and
were rescued by the police. On the same day a row took
place at the University over the ball, and soon became
general. Hysterical mothers and sweethearts forced
themselves into the rumpus and fainted, while children
were trampled underfoot. The unfinished match wasn’t
resumed.
* * * *
At a late football-match in N. Melb., an enthu-
siastic sympathiser with the visiting Collingwoods wrought
havoc with “ something wrapped up in brown paper.”
The “ something ” was generally believed—considering
the way in which men went down before it—to be an iron
bar. It turned out to be a copy of “Sweet Alice, Ben
Bolt.” The only wonder is that the injuries received from
this frightful weapon didn’t prove fatal.
* * * *
First-class Rugby in Sydney has already petered
out. The Wentworth-Randwick meeting this Saturday is
a challenge affair, and two sultry spells are expected. The
proceeds very properly go to an ambulance corps.
* * * *
The touring Queenslanders are striking bad
patches in M.L. One town put in a team averaging over
13st., and the visitors had to look on. M.L. athletes ap-
pear to be getting bigger every year.
» * » •
Gov. Brassey and his spouse were largely starred
as an additional attraction to the big South Melbourne v.
Collingwood match last Saturday, posters headed by their
portraits being profusely scattered over the city. Yet
“ Uncle Tom ” and Madam cleared out at half-time,
evidently disgusted at the game being so ladylike, after
all they had read of late.
* * * *
If the leading Melb. football teams had planned
an attempt to revive old-lime interest in the champion-
ship, they couldn’t have arranged last Saturday’s results
differently. But football frenzy is a thing of the past.
* * * *
Melb. senior football interest reached its height
on Saturday when S. Melb. played Collingwood, and Melb.
Essendon. At S. Melb. Gov. Brassey watched his first
game of Vic. football, the heaviness of which seemed to
sit flatly on his soul. Collingwood, after a hard, even
game, just managed to make a draw with South, who
scored 3g. 10b. to 3g. sb.
Melbourne, now about the best team playing
football down South, followed up last Saturday’s Colling-
wood victory by walking over Essendon. The Red-and-
Blacks, quite out of the hunt, greatly missed several of
their best (injured) players. Nevertheless, Melb.’s 7g. lib.
to Essendon’s sg. 3b. was well-deserved. Leith (Melb.)
kicked four splendid goals.
Port Melb. beat Fitzroy 5 to 3, and Carlton
scored similarly against St. Hilda.
Williamstown and Footsoray, after a rough
game, ended in a disgraceful riot. None of the players
attempted using the ball towards the finish, which con-
sisted of a series of more or less cowardly assaults. The
umpire removed the ball, stopped the game, and called in
the police. A decision will be given at the local police-
court during the week.
S. Melb. seem certain premiers for 1896.
* * * *
Itinerant photographers lose many money-
making chances by not patronising football. Pictures of
an angered crowd “ dealing it out ” to a mud-spattered
referee would sell well. Ditto reproductions of that lady
bashing an official during the interval at Melb. on Satur-
day. Also, negatives of an overjoyed barraeker kissing
the referee after a second-grade match in Sydney, a few
weeks back.

Fifty to one in hundreds have been booked about
Broken Hill for Melb. Cup. The length of the odds
proves that the layer doesn’t believe in the alleged sound-
ness of Broken Hill’s leg, for on his Australian Cup win
the handsome S.A. stayer is one of the best-handicapped
horses in the race. But the backer must have credit for
knowing something.

hope, and few backers were any the richer.

The fixing of a maximum handicap weight by
the A. J.C. is a desirable innovation. The minimum should
have been shifted up a few pounds also. “ Fluttering”
handicaps are a delusion and a snare, and astute owners
soon realise the fact.

* * * *
A tale of two Nationals: The M.L. horses
Tiritea and Donald M ‘Kinnon entered and accepted for
both M.L. and Caulfield G.N. Hurdles and Steeple double.
Billed to leave Auckland for Sydney, they didn’t, and
punters rushed them for the “locals.” Leaving, osten-
sibly for Christchurch, they stopped at Wellington, and
every paper in the country published conflicting state-
ments about them daily for a fortnight. Then two boxes
were rigged up on an Australian-bound steamer. Mean-
while they were being supported in Sydney and Melb. for
Caulfield, one man laying £lO,OOO. At the beginning of
Caulfield week Sydney Telegraph printed a wire from
Melb. saying the prads had arrived there. Next day-
scratched. Now it transpires they never went to Melb. at
all, having gone to Maoriland G.N. meet from Wellington.
When this was known, horseites there went a raker on
Donald M'Kinnon for the steeple, drawing their salaries
in advance to back him. Final: On the day, the uncon-
sciously-humorous stable, quiet and dumb-like, won in-
stead with Mutiny, a son of Mute.

* m # #
A novel billiard-match was played at a London
club recently. One player wore a suit of armour and ten
pairs of woollen gloves. The othey muffled his hands in
a pair of boxing gloves. The game was 1000 up, both
players starting at 980. The man with the “mittens”
won.
* * * *
Scotch blood always tells in shooting competi-
tions, though the average Caledonian prefers a fixed
target to a Hying one, as a rule. Donald MTntosh, the
best of Victorian wing-shots, is in a fair way to be recog-
nised as the champion pigeon-potter of Europe. At Hur-
lingham international meeting he met a choice collection
of shooters, all going for the big money prizes, and his
“bag” amounted to £5B3 —a fact that speaks for itself.
Also the name of the latest “ Australian” hero speaks for
itself.
* * • *
The Argus has been hoaxed once more, this
time by a veritable “ deeds that won the empire ” story
of a Homeric combat between a game-cock and a turkey
in the yard of the Melb. Detective Office. It all appeared
in large type, with the names of the party from whom the
turkey was stolen, and the detectives concerned, and
under special cross-headings. The Argus must have been
thinking of the days when the Duke of Edinburgh was in
Melbourne. At that time they did have cock-fights at the
Detective Office—in the" presence of H.R.H., and under
the auspices of the Police-commissioner himself.

One of the very best dressings for burns
is a compress soaked in fresh milk, renewed night
and morning.
Bandolph Bedford, in S.A. Chronicle,
puts in a good word for the much-abused Wes-
tralian Government:—
In Esperance I flud the first example in West Australia
of the desire to lean on the Government, and to be “agin
the Government ” if it refuses to act the leaning-post.
Three or four people arrive and make a settlement by
reason of the location being a goldfield, or a halting-place
on a goldfield’s track. On the track of the prospector
come the condenser man, and a gentleman who sells
summer drinks, or winter drinks for that matter, without
a license. Then come the regulation seller of malignant
intoxicants, and the man who has been a draper on the
east coast, but who becomes here a mining manager and
advisory expert. When the township is three months old
a loquacious person generally a barber —arrives with a
floating capital of eighteenpenee or thereabouts. By the
time his residence on the field aggregates a fortnight, the
responsibility of his investments causes him t.o form him-
self into a progress committee, which airs its wonderful
ignorance of the equalisation of finance by memorial-
ising the Government for an expenditure of £30,000 or
so on roads —the revenue of the settlement being £lOOO
or less a year. The native “ Westralians ” are now in the
position of the Boers, who—all jingo whooping being
beside the question—have a lot of justice on their side.
The floating population are the Uitlanders, and thus the
Transvaal is repeated in Westralia, only in a more peace-
ful style. The Westralian says: “We have our interests
bound in our old agricultural settlements. You t’other-
siders improve our position by your presence, but if your
new goldfield settlements prove failures large Govern-
ment expenditure will recoil, not on you, who will go
back east, but on us who have to stay here where are our
living and our dead—stay here to face the music and foot
the bill.” Despite all the abuse that has been levelled at
the Government, the fact remains that it has kept an
almost equal step with the wonderfully swift march of the
new population, and that it builds railways quicker than
any other country in the world.

Mr. Charles Stephenson, a well-known
Railway Employe at Kalapoi, New
Zealand, writes:
“About ten years ago, while en-
gaged in shunting, my loot caught
between the rails, and my leg was
fractured below the knee. It healed
in time, but 1 have been troubled
ever since with swollen veins, and
have been obliged, at times, to wear
a bandage. About a year ago it be-
came much worse, and I feared I
should be obliged to give up my
work. A friend advised me to try
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla. I did so, and
after taking four bottles the swell-
ing disappeared, and I have not
been troubled with it since.”

“ Oobdenism is on its defence.”—The
Foktnighty Review.
Sydney D.T. winds up a pathetic story of
a horse killed by a N.S.W. locomotive with the
emotional remark—“ He leaves a widow and
grown-up family.”
For a nice, large horror the experience of
the assistant man-butcher at a recent Newgate
(Eng.) execution would be hard to beat. When
the bolt was drawn the Crown Law officer did not
step back asjyromptly as he should have done, and
so fell through the trap into the pit; to save him-
self he clutched hold of the legs of the patient
and hung on like grim death.
The English companies must look to their
laurels. The North German Lloyd Co. will have
on the, Aus ralian line at the end of the year two
magnificent mailboats of Atlantic-liner speed-
each of 11,000 tons and 20,000 tons displacement,
and double-engined with triple-expansion engines
of 7000 h.p. These boats are to be fitted with
gorgeous passenger accommodation and all modern
comforts for tropical travel, including the most
luxurious forms of baths.
Liebknecht, leader of the German Social-
ists, writes of his recent lecturing visit to Eng-
land :—“For the first time since the downbreak
of the Chartist movement the masses of the Eng-
lish workingmen are stirring to fight in their own
cause. . . . I looked for Jingoism in England, and
I did not find it among the working classes. It is
in England, as it is in Germany, France, and
other countries, Jingoism or Chauvinism is the
invention and monopoly of the capitalistic classes,
whose interest it is to keep the working classes,
and to perpetuate international discord —the last
hope of capitalism.” But Bulletin experience
doesn’t tally with this. You may judge the Eng-
lishman at Home by the Englishman abroad — and
can’t the latter crawl !
While the sour Sabbatarians of other folds
are mouthing woes upon Narrabri (N.S.W.) foot-
ballers, and the like, the local R.C, priest sanctions
such arid other Sunday recreations, provided that
religious “duties” are not neglected. His
reverence specially mentions fishing, dancing,
bowling, and the making of wedding garments !
Shure, in good ould Oireland, it did be the Sunday
fashion, after Mass, for the bhoys and the colleens
to meet on the village-grane and dance forninst
aich other, each blioy, be the same token, giving
the fiddler a penny. If there was no fiddler, one
of the colleens would “ lal-lal, lal-lal-3al-la ” the
chune ! And even in dissenting areas of England,
the writer has seen the village youths “tussling”
(wrestling) on the grassed spaces on Sunday after-
noons.

A German savant, Seiiion of Jena, from a
study of Australian anthropology has just pub-
lished some interesting conclusions. Australian
blacks are ranked in culture-grade above the
Veddas of Ceylon, about equal to the Fuegians of
Patagonia, slightly lower than the Pigmies and
Bushmen of Africa, the aboriginal Brazilians of
S. America, and the Eskimos. The Australians and
Dravidians of India belong to the same main stem,
which sprang from a root including races like the
Ceylonese Veddas, the Japanese Ainus, and the
Khmers and Chains of Cambodia. The white or
Caucasian race, being of Dravidian descent (or
ascent), are thus more nearly related to the low
wild Australian black than to the higher Mongols,
Malays, or negroes. So the Queensland squatter
is more nearly related to his “ black boy ” than to
his Chinese or Javanese cook.

Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Herr Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later: —“ The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I

selves the right to accept or reject such subscriptions.
Subscriptions may be paid in full upon allotment, dividends accruing proportionately upon such
fully paid-up Shares from the date of payment.
In the event of no allotment being made, the application money will be returned in full.

The MEMORANDUM of ASSOCIATION provides —
*\ The Corporation is hereby precluded from investing in, or holding any portion of its Capital in
securities which have not at the date of investment a recognised market value, and which have not at
the date of acquisition paid dividends or interest to the holders regularly during the two years imme-
diately preceding (Government securities and priorities over dividend-paying companies excepted);
and no portion of its Capital may be invested in Mining Companies’ Shares.” It cannot, therefore,
participate in the formation of any new undertaking.
“ The Corporation is precluded from investing in or holding any portion of its Capital in securities
upon which the liability is unlimited.” ...
“ The Corporation is precluded from raising money upon Mortgage, receiving deposits at interest,
or issuing Capital having any priority whatever.”
PROSPECTUS.
THE purpose of the AUSTRALASIAN SECURITIES INVESTMENT CORPORATION,
Limited (which will be virtually a co-operation between shareholders in existing well-established
companies), is to satisfy one of the greatest wants of these colonies —a channel for investment in safe
and convertible securities which give adequate promise of a reasonable return, with a minimum of risk
and liability. Many classes of the community have neither the opportunity nor the experience to
enable them to properly gauge the value of the securities which the markets offer, and a Corporation
such as this affords scope for spreading capital over a great variety of investments throughout Aus-
tralasia, which private individuals with small means and little experience cannot hope to accomplish.
It is generally acknowledged that the financial institutions of Australasia will not be able to pay
the same rates of interest on deposits that have ruled in the past, and that mortgage-interest will be
correspondingly reduced ; and it is recognised that, though such rates may fluctuate, they must be
permanently on a lower basis than heretofore. These Colonies are being brought into closer connection
with the Old World, and financial conditions must consequently assimilate more nearly than they have
hitherto done.
There are over one hundred securities quoted upon the various Australasian Stock Exchanges,
which, during the crises, and since, have continued to pay steady and, in many instances, large divi-
dends. Although separately they are subject to the risks of individual management and to competi-
tion, it is considered that, taken collectively, they afford as substantial and profitable a mass of invest-
ment, combined with safety, as can be found in any country in the world.
THE AUSTRALASIAN SECURITIES INVESTMENT CORPORATION, Limited, is
founded on the principle of Mutual Insurance, and is restricted from investing more than 10 per cent,
of its capital as a maximum in any one of those existing securities, .this maximum being reduced as
further issues of capital are made until it ultimately reaches 6 per cent. But its purpose is to hold
permanently only comparatively small amounts in the capitals of as many of these steadily dividend-
paying undertakings as possible. The shares of the Corporation will therefore, it is believed, constitute
a security fulfilling the following essentials of a desirable, safe investment of the highest standing :
1. The utmost security of the principal.
2. A constant rate of interest above that now obtainable upon Government stock, bank deposits,
or high-class mortgages.
3. Absence of speculation.
4. Recognised and ready marketability, subject to a minimum of fluctuation in price.
5. No personal liability on the part of the investor after payment of his shares in full.
6. The prospect of a gradual increase in principal.
7. An absence of the risks attaching to particular industries, and individual management.
8. Possessing a capital based wholly upon long-established joint stock enterprise in all parts of
these colonies, and which it is believed cannot default short of an entire collapse in the
solvency of Australasia.
The maximum dividend payable by the Corporation is limited to 5 per cent, per annum, until the
whole authorised Capital has been paid up, and it is proposed that all additional profits realised during
this process shall accumulate and be re-invested. It is further provided that such investment of addi-
tional profits, after a reserve fund of 20 per cent, upon the Capital of the Corporation has been accu-
mulated, may be distributed amongst the shareholders in the form of allotments of new shares in the
Corporation. Thus, it is anticipated that the shareholders’ principal will gradually be increased, and
with that increase the interest received upon the original investment will expand. This principle
embodies in some degree the benefits accruing under a participating life insurance policy, but such
participation will possess the lasting advantage of not terminating at the death of the original holder.
The Directors may, at their discretion, retain upon current account, or on temporary deposit, with
their bankers, such a sum as they may consider advisable in the interests of the Corporation.
The preliminary expenses (which are limited to the usual legal charges and brokerage) will be dis-
tributed over the first five years ; and the expenses of management will be exceptionally small. The
Directors will be virtually Trustees for the shareholders, and all dividends received from investments
will we paid direct to the bankers of the Corporation, where they will remain until distributed under
authority from the Board. The Corporation is precluded from conducting any business whatever out-
side the defined limits.
It is intended to apply for a quotation on the Sydney and Melbourne Stock Exchanges ; and the
Directors are empowered to make additional issue of shares, either in Australasia or in London,
provided always that a preponderating proportion of the capital shall be retained upon the Australasian
register or registers.
The Board reserves the right to declare the Corporation formed upon £lOO,OOO Capital being
subscribed.
Prospectus and Form 3 of Application can be obtained from the Brokers, Messrs. JONES and
BLACK, Bond-street, Sydney, or at the HEAD OFFICE AND BRANCHES OF THE BANK
OF NEW SOUTH WALES, the Bankers to the Corporation.

A Bulletin correspondent :
Archbishop Carr’s school reading-books will, it is con-
fidently asserted, be a pleasant surprise from the stand-
point of Australasian interest. Australasian Govern- i
ments have been criminally doltish in the matter of pro- j
viding suitable literature for their youngsters, and a
hope, perhaps too sanguine, of the Archbishops is that
the literary excellence of his little books and their philan- j
thropic and liberal sentiments will secure their adoption
by one or more of the Australasian Governments. Any-
how, these new books amount to a flank attack on the
secular State which, with loud trumpeting, ousted, the
churches from the education of the young, and then
went placidly asleep as regards their intellectual food. j
In Vic. Assembly, under the new standing
orders, a bill which has been left unfinished can be
taken up next session where it was left off, instead
of being laboriously started again at the beginning.
The only necessary formula is the following choice
piece of obscurity which is read by the clerk of
the House :
I certify that this Bill is identical with a Bill as last
agreed to by the Legislative Assembly which passed its
second reading in the last preceding session of this present
Parliament, but was not finally disposed of by both
Houses when the session closed.
This is the true legal method of trying to get
everything into one long, tangled sentence. What
the pompous person really wants to say is : “I
certify that a bill was introduced during the last
session of this Parliament. That bill was not
finally disposed of by both Houses when the said
session closed. That bill is this bill.” Much
clearer, much Shorter.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
* “ The average barmaid,” writes a British
enquirer into the subject, “prefers her lot to that
of a lady help, or nursery governess.” Here and
there a superior Hebe gets tired of being “ a sorb
of decoy,” but the others are glad to occupy a
position where they “do see life, anyway.” Bub
life, in the full sense of the term, is not revealed to
barmaids. Their knowledge of. female character in
private domestic circles is nil; the men they meet
every day are masqueraders more or less. Where
does Hebe gather her ideas and pick up food for
reflection ? From her “ best ” customers, her
friends, as she calls them— i.e., her most frequent
customers, pub. loungers, bookmakers, the ‘ * flash ”
types of commercial and professional men. She
naturally listens with more interest to the conver-
sation of matured drinkers than to the chatter of
young johnnies, for these, as she soon learns from
experience, are very likely going to the dogs,
if not to jail. Mankind, as she sees it, is divided
into tipplers who conduct themselves decently,
and tipplers who are lewd beasts, both classes
being more or less englamoured by an artificial
cheerfulness. She is .apt to pity a blear-eyed
“ waster ” instead of despising him. He pitches
fairy tales to her across the bar of some apocryphal
faithless girl or wicked wife, whose conduct drove
him to the bottle for consolation. Her fund of
sympathy is exhausted upon disreputable Dicks
and Charleys—she has none left for worthy things.
Hebe seldom cherishes any real respect, or affec-
tion, for her “mashes,” because she is bound to
fool them ; it is her business to fool them,' to con-
ceal other flirtations from therii, to be false in little
matters if not a Jezebel at heart, and therefore her
womanly disposition to think tenderly of an ad-
mirer is largely overruled by the feeling that her
admirer is a “juggins.” She knows or
next to nothing, of inen who don’t come at least
six times' a week to squeeze her hand and talk to
her with at least one glass of drink in them. Her
world is enveloped in a mist of whisky fumes, her
days are passed in promoting stupid dissipation,
her eyes rove from one familiar customer to
another, when not engaged in watching for some
special “ friend ” who has promised to come in at
that particular time. And she flatters herself
that she sees life. Poor, blind Hebe !

This Invention, lately patented, revolutionises the ordi-

are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs. They

MELBOURNE-


According to Northern scientists, the rain-
fall at Enoggera reservoir (Brisbane) is evaporated
at the rate of a-quarter of an inch per diem. In
the interior of Australia an inch per day in
summer is about the average, and when one con-
siders that the yearly rainfall seldom exceeds (in
Western districts) lOin. or 12in., the awful signifi-
cance of the fact becomes strikingly apparent.
A. 0. Gregory tells how he once, while exploring,
left some hard india-rubber mugs on a table and
came back in a few hours to find a shapeless,
glutinous mass in place of the vessels. But then
a temperature of 118deg. will melt india-rubber,
and 125deg. in the shade is not uncommon in the
backblocks.
At the recent Melb. lecture on “Medical
Education,” a clergyman elicited a cheer from the
“ distinguished ” audience when he urged on doc-
tors that it was their duty to point out the im-
morality of keeping children at school for a long
stretch in the day-time and then piling weary
home-lessons on them at night. But when he was
logical enough (and manly enough) to refer to the
immorality of merchants compelling their tired
clerks to come back to the vitiated air of their
offices at night to save a few shillings, the “ dis-
tinguished” audience hissed and groaned in-
wardly, and was outwardly as demonstrative as a
block of ice.

Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent who says
that the umpire-stoushing at N. Melb. will put another
nail in the coffin of Vic. football is all out. Read the
future in the history of the past; and you will find that
the popularity of the game is in direct ratio to the spirit,
ill-feeling, brutality, or whatever you like to call it, that
its players and barraokers exhibit. Melbourne and Ade-
laide football was at its highest in ’B7 to ’9l, and, during
that period, club partizanship was never at so high a
pitch. Practically all the young men and youths of all
classes in both cities wore the colors of their favored clubs
daily, a practice that led to many a free fight, not alone
at matches but during the week. When was excitement
higher than in the deciding Geelong v. South match of ’B7
at the S. Melb. ground, yet for three weeks prior it was
freely rumored and believed that the South barraokers
intended to drown the Geelong players in Albert Park
lagoon adjoining the ground. Scarcely a Saturday passed
then without some “ disgraceful scene ” on one of the
grounds; umpires had repeatedly to escape by the back
doors of the pavilions to avoid being mangled by the in-
furiated mobs in front; and fighting on the playing-ground
was an accessory of about every third match. Anybody
who witnessed any of the old-time contests of Fpotscray
and Williamstown, Richmond and North, St. Hilda and
Prahran, or South and Ports, and saw road-metal and
pieces of quartering used to lay out objectionable
opponents, will recognise the fallacy of the contention
alluded to. —Larrikin.
* * * *
The captain of a North Melb. junior football
organisation was giving evidence in an umpire-stoushing
appeal case before Judge Casey at Melb. Criminal Court.
Asked by the opposing barrister “ What did you do when
the final bell sounded ? ” he replied, “I ran towards the
umpire.” “ And why did you do that ? ” I wanted to get
his name ; he gave such unfair decisions.” One to the
captain. But the barrister was not beaten. “ Did you
see him struck ?” he persevered. “ Yass, I seen a bloke
stoush him.” “Then, did you run up to protect him?”
The. witness stiffened upright for a second, then his jaw
dropped and his eyes started from his head ; leaning his
head forward, he repeated slowly and with an expression
of scorn actors would give years of study to acquire :
“ Did-I-run-up-ter-pertect-’im ? Me run up ter pertect
’im?”—a pause—“No-o-o-o”—deliberately—“ I did not
run up ter pertect ’im.” Even the Court smiled at the
emphasis of the reply, and the lawyer, gathering himself
for a final attack, observed : “ I suppose you thought he
did not receive sufficient punishment?” “ Well, ’e could
er done with a trifle more.” The witness then stood
down.
* * * *
To realise the complete possibilities of football,
writes a Bulletin correspondent, one must see a junior
match. The other Saturday 1 had the pain of witnessing
the W. Melb. juniors pulverise Hawthorn. Up to half-
time the game was fairish, but thence on, “slinging,”
tripping, even “rabbiting” and “stoushing” were in
order. As one barracker near me said to his mate, there
was “plenty of jog to-day.” “Yes,” returned he, “they’re
makin’ it blanky willin’.” After three-quarter-time the
barraokers —mostly Hawthorn —decided to take a hand ;
and strolled on to the ground, got in the way of their
friends’ opponents, tripped them, and made things
generally “ very willin’.” The umpire was there in person,
but he might as well have been away. It was a notable
exlrhition of our “ glorious winter game.”
• * * *
A husband and wife lately cycled from Aramac
to Normanton, 715 miles. Journey took 15 days, of which
three were devoted to rest, so that the average rate was
about 60 miles a day.
* * * *
A Brisbane stock and station agent recently
travelled 5000 miles in Western Queensland on the wheel,
at a cost of 11s. 6d. The same journey per buggy would
have cost some hundreds Way for the bike 1
* * * *
Two workmen employed in a Ohio (IJ.S.) cycle-
factory recently stole all the parts of a machine. At the
trial their lawyer showed that the entire value of the
stolen parts was under £3, and claimed that the co. which
sold the bike for £25 was the real thief. A discrimi-
nating jury admitted the logic and acquitted the
prisoners.
* * * *
Amongst the N.S.W. retrenched is Elias Lay-
cock, the ex-sculler, who was Inspector of Fisheries at
Port Hacking. He has taken to farming in the same
locality.
» • * *
Sydney Methodist-parson Geo. Martin is a
bikist (pants-inside-soeks, ooat-tails-hobbled), and says
that the bike “ is an instrument which may be largely
used in God’s service as well as in the devil’s.” So may
the tote.
* * * *
A severe blow is struck at English cycling by
the suspension of Michael, the 19-year-old Welsh laa,
pending enquiry into alleged cronk running in the recent
“ chain ” matches. If Michael be guilty, his offence is
ten times more heinous than that of the ordinary “dead”
cyclist or jockey. To receive a big sum from the Simpson
lever chain people to do his best for them and then
“turn dog” is an act repulsive to the most hardened
spieler. The state of cycling in France may be gauged
by the fact that the Parisian tracks decline to recognise
Miohael’s suspension, and still use him as a draw to their
meetings.

A cycle-caterer in a populous country district
offered a parson so much per year for the use of a fence
near the kirk door for a bike advt. Somebody’s incom-
parable horse-embrocation was cited as a precedent. The
impecunious elders are conseederin’ the matter.
* • * *
The Marylebone C.C., says a Sydney paper, is
one of the most exclusive clubs in the world. Yet
any first-class cricketer who travels as an amateur can
get elected a member of the M. 0.0. —Grace and Stoddy,
for instance, are not Personages in a sassiety way of
speaking. The Jockey Club is an exclusive affair, seeing
that some of the whitest and wealthiest of racing men
don’t stand a possible show of entering it.
* * * *
A member of the Australian Eleven, writing to
an Adelaide friend, alleges that the memorable score of 18,
all out, against M.C.C. was not altogether due to the
wicket and Pougher and Hearne. The Australians had
visited London Tivoli the night previous. Usually veil-
behaved, they had a night out, and paid for it next day
on a bad wicket.
* * * *
K. J. Key, capt. of the Surrey county eleven,
writing in the Strand Mao. for June, remarks that Aus-
tralian cricket is “ perfectly marvellous, considering the
small population, the absence of professionals who devote
their whole time to the game, and the fewness of matches
played.”

—I have long since branded myself as a cricket-fraud,

about.” Candid 1
* • . * »
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent, signing
himself “Australian Blow,” who accuses The Bulletin of
a British bias in its cricket comments, seems to form his
conclusion from the fact that he knows of no other Aus-
tralian paper which isn’t biassed the other way. The
Bulletin, I take it, tries to counteract the general
tendency both of cricket cables and local reasonings
therefrom. There is always something to be said for the
unpopular side of the case, but Australian newspapers, in
general, are careful not to say it. They confine them-
selves to suggestions of what might have been if some-
thing different had happened to the advantage of Our
Boys, and never, by any chance, draw attention to the
misfortunes of the Englishmen, who must have their share
of bad luck sometimes. As regards the stupid Hinglish
excuse that test-matches are “sold” by the professional
players, it arises from the not infrequent evidence of pro-
fessionals’ indifference to “honor and glory” in minor
matches, where they are not under the observation of
their club authorities. For my part, I can swear that I’ve
heard many young Australians, as well as new-chums, say
of Stoddart’s last visiting team —“They could do what
they liked with our fellows,” though it was always ap-
parent that the visitors were trying their hardest in the
important games. Nevertheless, their last performance
in Melbourne—an extra match against Victoria, for some
benefit purpose—showed how badly the pros, can cut up
when they have no personal credit, or gate-money at
stake, and the captain happens to be ill. —Yours, British
Bombast.
* * * »
Dr. Pat. Moloney, of Melbourne, lecturing on
sport, declares we are cricketers, “ because our tree-
climbing ancestors tossed the fruit to their young, who
gleefully caught it.” This should make us more lenient
in regard to the monkey-tricks of various players at ’Ome.
* * * *
Mr. “ Johnny ” Deeble promised, at the start, to
rule the crooked Sydney trotting-owners with a rod of iron,
but he has hardly succeeded in carrying out his promise.
One stepper, “thrown in” week after week, runs a “bye”
without sign from the stewards. This ruins a race, as
owners, who might otherwise back their horse, stand off
for fear it may be that stepper’s day out.
* « * ♦
The American papers talk seriously of the pros-
pect of sending over a trotter or pacer to beat the great
N.S.W. horse Fritz. A writer in the Horseman suggests
a horse named Klatmath, with a 2.8 J record, as likely to
do the trick. Fritz, on our courses, can trot a mile in at
least 2.10, which means that it is 1000 to 1 against a horse
who, on perfect American courses, can trot a mile in 2.8 J,
crossing the water and beating 2.10 in Australia. No
horse has ever trotted in Australia within any number of
seconds of his American record. Mr. Foster bought the
pacer J.H. at a big figure, on the understanding that he
did a certain trial, and he carried out the contract. But
when J.H. arrived in Australia his American record had
been left behind, and he never recovered it.
• » * *
M.L. trotting has a better chance of reaching a
higher plane now that dissenting associations have shaken
hands. Colonial Sec. Carroll has promised the sport a
fair share of tote permits. But cronkness must disappear
from all racing. Weak-kneed clubs will forfeit their right
to race. Mr. Carroll’s plan is to treat them like publicans:
black marks, extinction.
• * * *
M.L. ring’s petition to bet on courses contains
one fairly sound argument. Owners backing at fixed
odds would receive those odds. At present, if they in-
vested on the tote at, say, sixes and their horses touched
threes, they would receive threes. Which is quite true.
But clubs say the machine gives better odds all-round,
and quote occasional mammoth divs. The books say
they would probably re-invest some of their big corns, on
the tote ; the club would gain, and owners, having got a
fixed price, wouldn’t be prejudiced. This, of course,
would favor owners, who could also play up on the nod.
One big club does license its local men, but the writer has
never seen their odds quoted alongside the tote’s in race-
reports.
* * • *
It is to be hoped the intended efforts of A. J.C.
committee to achieve the tote will prove successful.
Prevailing influences have reduced racing to a mere
open-robbery pastime ; the abolition of the noisy horde of
bookies, alleged bookies, swindlers, and hangers-on -would
be a public relief. The automatic layer doesn’t knowingly
fatten on “ stiffs,” nor does it lay scratches, or horses not
on the ground. Also, owners would benefit by added
values, and the public wouldn’t miss a small tax on their
pleasure for charities. As the gilded crowd will button-
hole vigorously in anticipation of the special meeting to
discuss machine legislation, members are hereby asked to
ponder upon the certainty of Sydney Cup being raised to
greater value than Melb. Cup in a few years ; of the estab-
lishment of a big winter National; of increased values all
round, encouraging outside competition and public pat-
ronage, and of the attainment of a higher standard of
turf morality than at present exists—or doesn’t exist.
» * * *
If the controlling powers are sincere in their
desire to restrict racing in the metropolitan area, the
legalisation of the tote would afford them opportunity.
The limitation of permits, and the interdiction of clubs
not getting them, would curb the evil right away. The
all-too-numerous shandygaff meetings got off on suburban
run-rounds weekly are quite unnecessary. They are
steeped in cronkness, and the proprietaries line their
pockets instead of improving their tracks and appurten-
ances.
* * * *
The odds fraternity are said to be quite within
their rights fielding against dead ’uns or non-starters.
The public trust to newspaper information re the lattei,
and fall in badly. At least six horses mentioned weekly
in Cup quotations aren’t in training and won’t be trained.
Yet all have been alluded to in rosy language, and
marked “dangerous,” especially in the allegedly well-
informed sporting papers which don’t seem to trouble
about private enquiries. Ikey Mo is a trifle more careful.
He knows when the “ handsome, well-bred full brother to
So-and-so ” is scampering about the paddock, and lays
accordingly.
* * * *
Fairfield, the Victorian veteran show-fencer,
won the jumping-contest at Brisbane, clearing (ift. 2in.
* * * *
Mr. Chaffey Baker, chairman of S.A.J.C., says
the totalisator has made his club “ the most successful
and prosperous jockey club in Australia.”
The multiplicity of readied-up pony “ pots ”
lately has caused much relief to bookies’ cash-bags. One
badly-stricken penciller asked a steward recently why
some of these inconsistent beauties weren’t brought to
grass. No one yells louder for “straight racing” than
the gent heavily hit by a “ waiter.” “ The expected hap-
pened at last,” said one of the papers the other day,
alluding to a long-deferred win. It happened in a big
field of well-backed ’uns, and the ring did not expect it
until the last moment, when money came at them all
roads, and was poured into every shop in the city.
* * * *
Many of Sydney’s blooded miniatures are
brought down from breeding stations in mobs, realising
£lO and upwards at auction. The over-height neddies
command better prices, because they seem to get under
the standard easier.
* * * *
An innocent country farmer wrote an old-time
acquaintance (now a pony-trainer), asking if a small pony
had a winning chance in Sydnej’. The miniature Carbine
proved to be a Shetland, little bigger than a Newfound-
land ! The trainer is trying to keep it as a curio. The
other night it was discovered sleeping in the kitchen with
the cat.
* * » *
Member Crick’s proposed N.S.W. <£lo stallion-
tax is badly required. The country is overrun with scrub-
bers allowed to perpetuate their species. The £3 tax on
racers is something new, but churchy Ms.P. may support
it on principle. Some of them would tax stakes offered
by clubs for competition, if they got a chance.
* * * *
Crick, M.P., once had a betting-case before a
certain judge, whose ignorance of sporting matters was
as apparent as his desire not to appear ignorant of them.
It was a law-suit over the payment of a double, and a
sporting witness was asked—“ Did the first horse win ? ”
when the judge interposed and exclaimed, testily, “Now,
Mr. Crick, do you think I am so ignorant as not to know
that the first horse always wins ? ” Later on the witness
was asked—“ Did the second horse win?” (meaning the
second race, of course.) The judge said, “ Sir, do you
suppose I’m such a fool as not to know that the second
horse cannot win a race? Of course your answer is
obvious, witness. Did the second horse win?" “No,
yer honor,” replied the witness ; “he was dead 1 ”
“ Dead,” said the judge, “ how could a dead horse run a
race? What do you mean?” “I mean he was ‘stiff,’”
said the witness. Whereupon the judge looked fierce,
threatened the witness with contempt of court, and bade
him stand down, talking nonsense about dead and stiff
horses running in races.
* • • *
A prize-winner at the recent Olympic Games
writes that the fame of young Flack’s athletic perform-
ances having preceded him to Athens, the committee of
the games became fearful of the chance of a Greek pulling
off the race from Marathon. Accordingly, they worked
the oracle in a fashion that would have done credit to an
Australian pony-stable. Entries for the great race closed
for Greeks a fortnight before the meeting, and a trial race
had been run under the direction of the authorities of the
sports to select the most likely local men. In order to
“dish” the foreign competitors, the committee ordered
another trial race to be run and sent up several more
competitors, one of whom ultimately proved successful in
the race itself. Post-entries for this second body were
accepted, in distinct violation of the advertised conditions.
Under these circumstances, it is a wonder that Australian
Flack, or the Americans, got a show at all.

At Inglewood (Vic.) the other day an old
fellow of 03 brought his grey hairs in sorrow to the
grave by deliberately blowing the top of his skull
off with a gun.
A Melbourne unemployed went into the
Russell-street police barracks and shot himself dead
the other evening. It seemed that the poor fellow
was anxious to give as little trouble as possible.
He took with him enough money to pay funeral
expenses, aiid remembering that the police would
have to convey his body to the morgue, he left it
with the police, to spare anyone the job of going
to fetch it.
A remarkable statement appears in a
privately-printed pamphlet just issued in Eng-
land, by Canon Jenkins, Lyminge, who corres-
ponded for many years with Cardinal Manning.
The cannon, in conversation with Manning after
the latter’s elevation to the cardinalate, referred
to the iniquity of the Papacy of the Renaissance
period, such as the doings of the Borgia, the
Ghisleri, and the Pamphli Papacies. The Car-
dinal seemed in complete ignorance of these chap-
ters of history, and heard of them not only with
horror but terror. He said to his Anglican friend :
“ My only comfort is that I believe in the Holy
Catholic Church. ” To which his friend replied :
“ But what if it is not holy ? ”
Dear Bulletin,— Be that politician’s snores
disturbing the swallows domiciled on a Northern
river - boat. The oldest 25-footer on Brisbane
waters—the Vagabond—belonged to me, and in
the ’9O flood two swallows built their nest on the
strop of the centre-board halyard, making it just
close to the mast and the end of the centre-board
case. We used to make fortnightly trips to More-
ton Bay, during which period the swallows lived
in undisturbed possession and hatched the eggs.
Out of two broods, however, we could only save
one young one. The parent-birds would follow us
down-stream for a couple of miles, then return to
the moorings and await the coining back of the
“ Vag.” This is no stretcher of a yarn, but a fact
which plenty of peoole will verify for you.—Fiz,
Brisbane.

Bess.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Supper was over at Shingle Hut and we
Were all seated round the tire —all except Joe. He
was mousing. He stood on the sofa with one ear
to the wall in a listening attitude and brandished
a table-fork. There were mice—mobs of them—
between the slabs and the paper layers of news-
papers that had been pasted one on the other at
periods ranging over ten years until they were an
inch thick ; and whenever Joe located a mouse he
drove the fork into the wall and pinioned it—or
reckoned he did.
Dad sat pensively at one corner of the fire-place
- Dave at the other with his elbows on his knees
and his chin resting in his palms.
“Think could you ride a race, Dave?” asked
Dad.
“Yairs,” answered Dave, without taking his
eyes off the fire, or his chin from his hands—
“'could, I suppose, if I’d a pair o lighter boots ’n
these.”
Again they reflected.
Joe triumphantly held up the mutilated form of
a murdered mouse and invited the household to
“ look.” No one heeded him.
“ Would your mother’s go on you ? ”
“ Might,” and Dave expectorated into the
fire.
“ Anyway,” Dad went on, * we must have a go
at this handicap with the old mare ; it’s worth
trying for, and, believe me, now, she’ll surprise a
few of their flash hacks, will Bess.”
“ Yairs, she can go all right.” And Dave spat
again into the fire.
“ Go / I’ve never known anything to keep with
her. Why, bless my soul, seventeen years ago,
when old Redwood owned her, there wasn’t a
horse in the district could come within cooee of
her. AJI she wants is a few feeds of corn and a
galiop or two, and mark my word she’ll show some
of them the way.”
* - * » *
Some horse-races were being promoted by
the shanty-keeper at the Overhaul—seven miles from
our selection. They were the first of the kind held
in the district, and the stake for the principal event
was =65. It wasn’t because Dad was a racing
man or subject to turf hallucinations in any way
that he thought of preparing Bess for the meet-
ing. We sadly needed those five pounds, and, as
Dad put it, if the mare could only win it would
be an easier and much quicker way of making a
bit of money than waiting for a crop to grow.
* * * *
Bess was hobbled and put into a two-acre
paddock near the house. We put her there be-
cause of tier wisdom. She was a chestnut, full ot
villainy, an absolutely incorrigible old rogue. If,
at any time, she was wanted when m the grass
paddock, it required the lot of us from Dad down
to yard her, as well as the dogs, and every other
do * - in the neighborhood. Not that she had any
brumby element in her—she would have been
pasier to yard if she had but she would drive
Steadily enough, alone or with other horses, until
she saw the yard, when she would turn and deli-
berately walk away. If we walked to head her
she beat us by half a length ; if we ran she ran,
and stopped when we stopped. I hat was the
aggravating part of her ! When it was only to go
to the store or the post-office that we required her,
we could have walked there and back a dozen'
times ere we could have run her down, but; some-
how, we generally preferred to labor in catching
her rather than walk.
When we had spent half the day hunting for
the curry-comb, which we didn’t find, Dad began
to rub Bess down with a corn-cob--a shelled one —
and trim her up a bit. He pulled her tail and cut
the hair off her heels with a knife ; then he gave
her some corn to eat, and told J oe he was to have
a bundle of thistles cut for her every night. Now
and again, while grooming her, Dad would step
back a few paces and look upon her with pride in
his eye.
“ There’s great breeding in the old mare,” he
would say —“ great breeding ; look at the shoulder
on her, and the loin she has ; and where did ever
you before see a horse with the same nostril ? Be-
lieve me, she’ll surprise a few of them.”
We began to regard Bess with profound respect;
hitherto we had been accustomed to pelt her
with potatoes and blue-metal.
The only thing likely to prejudice her chance in
the race, Dad reckoned, was a small sore on her
back about the size of a foal’s foot. She had had
that sore for upwards of ten years to our know-
ledge, but Dad hoped to have it cured before the
race came off with a never-failing remedy he had
discovered —burnt-leather and fat.
* * * *
Every day, along with Dad, we would
stand on the fence near the house to watch Dave
gallop Bess from the bottom of the lane to the
barn—about a mile. We could always see him
start, but immediately after he would disappear
down a big gully and we would see nothing more
of the gallop till he came within a hundred yards
of us. And wouldn’t Bess bend to it once she got
up the hill and fly past with Dave in thfe stirrups
watching her shadow ! —when there was one : she
was a little too fine to throw a shadow always.
And when Dave and Bess had got back and Joe
had led her round the yard a few times, Dad
would rub the corn-cob over her again and apply
more burnt-leather and fat to her back.
* * * *
On the morning preceding the race Dad
decided to send Bess over three miles to improve
her wind. Dave took her to the crossing at the
creek—supposed to be three miles from Shingle
Hut, but it might have been four or it might' have
been five, and there was a stony ridge on the way.
We mounted the fence and waited. Tommy
Wilkie came along riding a plough-horse. He
waited too.
“ Ought to be coming now,” Dad observed, and
Wilkie got excited. He said he would go and
wait in the gully and race Dave home. “ Race
him home ! ” Dad chuckled, as Tommy cantered
off, “ he’ll never see the way Bess goes.” Then
we all laughed.
Just as someone cried “Here he is !” Dave
turned the corner into the lane, and Joe fell off
the fence and pulled Dad with him. Dad damned
him and scrambled up again as fast as he could.
After a while longer Tommy Wilkie hove in sight
’midst a cloud of dust. Then came Dave, scarcely
faster than a trot, and flogging all he knew with a
piece of greenhide plough-rein. Bess was all-out
and floundering. There was about two hundred
yards yet to cover. Dave kept at her—thud !
thud ! Slower and slower she came. “Damn the
fellow ! ” Dad said ; “ what’s he beating her for?”
“ Stop it, you fool! ” he shouted. But Dave sat
down on her for the final effort and applied the
hide faster and faster. Dad crunched his teeth.
Once—twice—three times Bess changed her stride;
• then struck a branch root of a tree that projected
a few inches above ground, and over she went —
crash ! Dave, fell on his head and lay spread out,
motionless. We picked him up and carried him,
inside at once, and whevi mother saw blood on him
she fainted straight off without first waiting to
know if it were his own or not. Both looked as
good as dead, hut Dad, with a bucket of water, ;
soon brought them round again,
* * * *
It was scarcely dawn when we began pre-
paring for a start to the races. Dave, after
spending fully an hour trying in vain to pull on
mother’s elastic-side boots, decided to ride m Ins
own heavy bluchers. We went with Dad in the
dray. Mother wouldn’t go ; she said she didn’t
want to see her son get killed, and warned Dad
that if anything happened the blame would for
ever be on his head. .
We arrived at the Overhaul in good time. Dad
took the horse out of the dray and tied him to a
tree. Dave led Bess about, and we stood and
watched the shanty-keeper unpacking gingerbeer.
Joe asked Dad for sixpence to buy some, but Dad
hadn’t any small change. We remained there,
though (in front of the booth), most of the day,
and ran after any corks that popped out and
handed them in again to the shanty-keeper. He
didn’t offer us anything—not a thing !
“ Saddle up for the Overhaul Handicap ” was at
last sung out, and Dad, saddle on arm, advanced
to where Dave was walking Bess about. They
saddled up and Dave mounted, looking as pale as
death.
“I don’t like ridin’ in these boots a bit, he
said, with a quiver in his voice.
“ Wot’s up with ’em ? ” Dad asked.
“ They’re too big altogether.”
“Well, take ’em off, then ! ”
Dave jumped down and pulled them off—leav-
ing his socks on.
More than a dozen horses went out, and when
th.e starter said “ Off ” didn’t they go ! Our eyes
at once followed Bess. Dave was at her right
from the jump, the very contrary to what Dad
had told him. In the first furlong she put fully
20 yards of daylight between herself and the field
—she came after the field. At the back of the
course you could see the . whole of Kyle’s selection
and two of Jerry Keefe’s haystacks between her
and the others. We didn’t follow her any further.
After the race was won and they had cheered
the winner, Dad wasn’t to be found anywhere.
Dave sat on the grass quite exhausted. “Ain’t
y’ goin’ to pull the saddle off ? ” Joe asked.
“No,” be said; “I ain’t. You don’t want
everyone to see her back, do you ? ”
Joe wished he had sixpence.
* * * *
About an hour afterwards Dad came stag-
gering along arm-in-arm with another man—an
old fencing-mate of his, so he made out.
“Thur yai,” he said, taking off his hat and
striking Bess on the rump with it; “ besh bred
mare in the worl’.”
The fencing-mate looked at her, but didn’t say
anything ; he couldn’t.
“Eh?” Dad went on; “say sh’ain’t? L’ere —
every’ name is—betcher pound sh’is.”
Then a jeering and laughing crowd gathered
round, and Dave wished he hadn’t come to the
races.
“ She ain’t well,” said a tall man to Dad—
“short in her gallops.” Then a short, bulky indi-
vidual without whiskers shoved his face up into
Dad’s and asked him if Bess was a mare or a cow.
Dad became excited, and only that old Andei 1 -
son came forward and took him away there must
have been a row.
Anderson put him in the dray and drove it
home to Shingle Hut.
* * * *
Dad reckons now that there is nothing in
horse-racing, and declares it a fraud. He says,
further, that an honest man, by training and
racing a horse, is only helping to feed and fatten
the rogues and vagabonds that live on the sport.
Queensland.
